Divine Transcendence
beginner
5 min
Arguments 1–3: Istiwa', Fawq, and Uluww
Enumeration of the first three Quranic arguments for direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three Quranic arguments that anthropomorphists use to claim Allah has a direction, based on verses of istiwa', fawq, and uluww.

Core Idea
Three families of verses that seem to place Allah spatially above creation.

Al-Razi begins his survey of anthropomorphic arguments by presenting the most obvious ones: verses that literally mention sitting on the Throne, being above, or being high. These provide the foundation for their claim that Allah occupies a direction. This lesson covers the first three of nine arguments, each relying on a specific Arabic word or phrase.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for argument 1–3
Read the original Arabic for the first, second, and third arguments. Notice the repeated words: istiwa', fawq, and uluww.
The third argument contains multiple verses.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the three technical terms: istiwa', fawq, and uluww. Understand what each literally suggests.
3
Consider the anthropomorphic reading
Ask yourself: if taken literally, what would these verses imply about Allah? How might an anthropomorphist use them?
This sets up the refutation later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثلاثون
في
ما يتمسكون به في إثبات الجهة لله تعالى
تمسكوا في ذلك بالقرآن والأخبار والمعقول والوجوه المركبة من السمع والعقل (1)
أما القرآن: فمن عشرة أوجه:
الأول: التمسك بالآيات السنة الواردة بلفظ الاستواء على العرش (2).
الثاني: التمسك بالآيات المشتملة على لفظ الفوق. فقد قال تعالى: (وهو القاهر فوق عباده وهو الحكيم الخبير) (3)، وقال: (وهو القاهر فوق عباده ويرسل عليكم حفظة) (4)، وقال: (يخافون ربهم من فوقهم) (5).
الثالث: الآيات المشتملة على لفظ العلو. كقوله تعالى: (وهو العلي العظيم) (6)، وقوله تعالى: (وهو العلي الكبير) (7)، وقوله: (سبح اسم ربك الأعلى) (8)، وقوله: (إلا ابتغاء وجه ربه الأعلى) (9)، وأيضا: تواتر النقل في قوله تعالى: (سبحان الأعلى) (10).
الرابع: الآيات المشتملة على لفظ المروح إليه والصعود. قال تعالى: (تعرج الطيب) (11) (الملائكة والروح) (11) إليه، وقال: (إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب) (12).
الخامس: الآيات المشتملة على لفظ الإنزال والتنزيل. قالوا: وهي كثيرة تزيد على المائتين في حق القرآن المبين، والروح والملائكة المقربين والتوراة والإنجيل.
السادس: الآيات المقرونة بحرف إلى، مع أنها لانتهاء الغاية منها: قوله تعالى: (إلى ربها ناظرة) (13)، وذلك يقتضي انتهاء النظر إليه. وقوله: (ثم إلى ربكم ترجعون) (14)، وقوله: (وإلى المصير) (15)، وقوله: (ارجعي إلى ربك) (16).
السابع: قوله تعالى: (كلا إنهم عن ربهم يومئذ لمحجوبون) (17)، والحجاب إنما يصح في حق من يكون جسما، وفي جهة، حتى يصير محجوبا بسبب شيء آخر.
الثامن: الآيات الدالة على أنه في السماء. قال: (أم أمنتم من في السماء) (18)، وقال: (قل: لا يعلم من في السموات والأرض الغيب إلا الله) (19).
التاسع: الآيات المشتملة على الرفع إليه. قال تعالى في حق عيسى عليه السلام: (إني متوفيك ورافعك إلي) (20)، وقوله: (وما قتلوه بقنا).
Chapter Thirty: On what they cling to in establishing direction for Allah. They cling to the Qur'an, traditions, and rational arguments, and composite arguments from scripture and reason. As for the Qur'an: from ten aspects. First: clinging to the verses that came with the word 'istiwa' upon the Throne (2). Second: clinging to the verses containing the word 'above' (al-fawq). For He said: (And He is the Subduer above His servants, and He is the Wise, the Aware) (3), and He said: (And He is the Subduer above His servants, and He sends guardians over you) (4), and He said: (They fear their Lord from above them) (5). Third: verses containing the word 'highness' (al-‘uluww). Like His saying: (And He is the Most High, the Great) (6), and His saying: (And He is the Most High, the Great) (7), and His saying: (Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High) (8), and His saying: (except seeking the face of his Lord, the Most High) (9), and also the transmitted frequent reports of His saying: (Glorify the Most High) (10). Fourth: verses containing the word 'al-marwaḥ' (ascension?) to Him and rising. He said: (ascends the good) (11) (the angels and the Spirit) (11) to Him, and He said: (To Him ascends the good word) (12). Fifth: verses containing the word 'descending' (al-inzal) and 'sending down' (al-tanzil). They said: They are many, exceeding two hundred concerning the manifest Qur'an, and the Spirit, and the near angels, and the Torah, and the Gospel. Sixth: verses connected with the preposition 'ila' (to), although it is for the limit of the goal. His saying: (Looking towards her Lord) (13), and that necessitates the end of looking to Him. And His saying: (Then to your Lord you will be returned) (14), and His saying: (And to the destination) (15), and His saying: (Return to your Lord) (16). Seventh: His saying: (Nay, on that Day they will be veiled from their Lord) (17). And the veil is only valid in regard to one who is a body and in a direction, so that he becomes veiled by something else. Eighth: verses indicating that He is in heaven. He said: (Or are you secure from Him who is in the heaven) (18), and He said: (Say: None in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah) (19). Ninth: verses containing the word 'raising' (al-raf‘) to Him. He said concerning Jesus, peace be upon him: (I will take you and raise you to Me) (20), and He said: (And they did not kill him with certainty).
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a classification of nine Quranic arguments used by those who affirm a literal direction (jiha) for Allah. Al-Razi lists them under a heading that also mentions traditions and rational arguments, but here only the Quranic ones are detailed. He states that there are ten aspects in total, but only nine are present in this excerpt. Each argument cites specific verses that anthropomorphists take as proof that Allah is in a direction, such as being above, in heaven, or having a location. The first argument invokes verses about istiwa' (sitting upon the Throne). The second uses verses containing the word 'above' (fawq). The third uses verses with 'highness' (uluww). The fourth, though the Arabic is unclear, likely refers to verses about ascension (mi'raj). The fifth notes the large number of verses about sending down revelation as proof of above-ness. The sixth employs verses using the preposition 'to' (ila) to imply a goal or direction. The seventh uses the verse about being veiled (hijab) from Allah on the Day of Judgment, arguing that veiling only applies to bodies in a place. The eighth uses verses saying He is 'in the heaven'. The ninth uses verses about Jesus being raised to Allah. Al-Razi is here presenting the opposition's case, which he will later refute.

Key Terms
الاستواء
الفوق
العلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these verses prove Allah literally sits on a throne or is physically above creation.
Correction: The Ash'ari position interprets these verses metaphorically, affirming Allah's transcendence above spatial location.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the first three arguments listed by al-Razi?
A) Verses of istiwa' upon the Throne
B) Verses using the word 'above' (fawq)
C) Verses using the word 'highness' (uluww)
D) Verses using the word 'descending' (inzal)

Key Takeaway
The first three anthropomorphic arguments rely on verses using 'istiwa', 'fawq', and 'uluww' to suggest a spatial direction for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses like 'He is the Subduer above His servants,' do you feel that Allah is spatially above you, or do you understand it as a metaphor for His authority? How does your answer affect the way you humble yourself in prayer?
?
If you were asked to defend the Ash'ari interpretation of these verses to someone who insists on a literal direction, would you be able to do so using the very verses listed in this lesson? What would you say, and does your current understanding equip you to answer confidently?
?
The verses about 'istiwa' and 'uluww' describe Allah's majesty. When you read them, do they inspire awe and reverence, or do they create confusion about His nature? How can you transform that confusion into deeper reliance on the scholars of tawil?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

Arguments 4–6: Ascension, Descending, and the Preposition 'Ila'
Enumeration of arguments 4–6: ascension, descending, and the preposition 'ila'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the next three Quranic arguments used to claim Allah has a direction: those involving ascension, descending, and the preposition 'ila' indicating a goal.

Core Idea
Verses about movement toward or from Allah imply He is in a specific place.

Moving from static spatial terms, the next three arguments focus on verses that describe movement: things ascending to Allah, things descending from Him, or the preposition 'to' marking a direction. These seem to imply a spatial endpoint. Al-Razi lists them as the fourth, fifth, and sixth proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for arguments 4–6
Read the Arabic carefully. Note the words 'al-marwah', 'al-inzal', and the use of 'ila'.
The fourth argument contains a typo 'al-marwah', likely meaning 'ascension'.
2
Identify the logic behind each argument
For each, ask: how does the verse suggest direction? Ascension implies an upward direction; descending implies from above; 'ila' implies a goal.
3
Consider the cumulative weight
Anthropomorphists claim these many verses cannot all be metaphorical. Note the large number (over 200) for the fifth argument.
This builds their case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
How many verses does al-Razi (quoting the anthropomorphists) claim use the word 'inzal' or 'tanzil'?
A) Over 100
B) Over 200
C) Over 300

Key Takeaway
Arguments 4–6 exploit verses about ascension, descending, and the preposition 'ila' to argue that Allah has a spatial direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'to your Lord you will be returned', do you instinctively picture a physical journey? How does that mental image shape your understanding of the afterlife, and what would it mean if that journey is not spatial but existential?
?
Have you ever insisted on a literal interpretation of a Quranic verse (like 'He descends') because it felt comfortable, ignoring the possibility of deeper meaning? Are you willing to let go of that comfort for the sake of tanzīh?
?
If Allah is not in any direction, how does that affect your prayer—do you still feel you are facing Him? Is your relationship with Him dependent on imagining a location, or can it transcend space?

Arguments 7–9: Veil, In Heaven, and Raising
Enumeration of arguments 7–9: veil, in heaven, and raising.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final three Quranic arguments for Allah's direction: the veil verse, verses saying He is in heaven, and the raising of Jesus.

Core Idea
Three more specialized verses that seem to imply a physical location for Allah.

The last three arguments are more specific: the verse about being veiled from Allah on the Day of Judgment, which only makes sense if Allah is a body in a place; verses stating 'He is in heaven'; and verses about Jesus being raised to Allah. Al-Razi presents these as further Quranic proof for direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for arguments 7–9
Read the original Arabic. The seventh argument mentions 'hijab', the eighth 'fi al-sama', the ninth 'raf'.
2
Understand the logic of the veil argument
The argument claims that veiling (hijab) only applies to bodies in a direction. If Allah can be veiled, He must be a body in a place.
This is a key anthropomorphic inference.
3
Note the final argument is incomplete
The passage ends mid-sentence for the ninth argument. Recognize that this is due to the page break in the source.
The original lesson continues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Each argument cites specific verses that anthropomorphists take as proof that Allah is in a direction, such as being above, in heaven, or having a location. The first argument invokes verses about istiwa' (sitting upon the Throne). The second uses verses containing the word 'above' (fawq). The third uses verses with 'highness' (uluww). The fourth, though the Arabic is unclear, likely refers to verses about ascension (mi'raj). The fifth notes the large number of verses about sending down revelation as proof of above-ness. The sixth employs verses using the preposition 'to' (ila) to imply a goal or direction. The seventh uses the verse about being veiled (hijab) from Allah on the Day of Judgment, arguing that veiling only applies to bodies in a place. The eighth uses verses saying He is 'in the heaven'. The ninth uses verses about Jesus being raised to Allah. Al-Razi is here presenting the opposition's case, which he will later refute.

Key Terms
الحجاب
السماء
الرفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the veil verse proves Allah is a physical body in a direction.
Correction: Ash'ari theologians interpret 'hijab' as a metaphorical veiling, not a physical barrier.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why do anthropomorphists use the veil verse (Q 83:15) as proof of direction?
A) Because the verse mentions a physical veil
B) Because veiling only applies to bodies in a direction
C) Because the verse says Allah is in heaven
D) Because the verse uses the word 'above'

Key Takeaway
Arguments 7–9 use the concepts of veiling, being in heaven, and raising to Jesus to argue that Allah occupies a spatial direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear or recite verses like 'He is in the heaven,' do you find yourself unconsciously forming a mental image of Allah in a specific place? What does that reveal about your understanding of tawheed, and what concrete step can you take today to purify your belief of any trace of anthropomorphism?
?
The ninth argument uses the raising of Jesus to claim that Allah is 'above.' Have you ever felt comforted by thinking of Allah as 'up there' rather than as encompassing all without location? How might this subtle comfort actually be a veil over the true majesty of Allah's transcendence, and what will you do to remove that veil from your heart?
?
The seventh argument suggests that a veil only makes sense for a physical being. When you read that the disbelievers are veiled from their Lord, do you feel a sense of relief that you are not veiled, or do you realize that any mental image of Allah as a body is itself a veil? What change in your daily worship will you make to ensure you are not veiled by your own imagination?

Ten Quranic Proofs for Direction and the Claim of Clarity
Imam Razi lists seven categories of Quranic verses used by anthropomorphists and their argument for clarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how anthropomorphists use Quranic verses to argue for Allah having a direction, and why they claim these verses are clear and unambiguous.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists cite abundant Quranic verses to claim direction, asserting clarity by early silence.

Imam Razi presents the anthropomorphists' case by listing seven categories of Quranic verses they use to argue that Allah has a direction. They then argue that these verses are clear (muhkama) because they are numerous and early scholars never reinterpreted them. Understanding their proof is essential before engaging with Razi's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the structure of the anthropomorphists' argument become clear.
Notice that the passage enumerates categories from fourth to tenth, indicating a larger list.
2
Identify each category of verses
Go through categories 4 to 10. For each, note the specific theme (ascent, sending down, 'to', veiling, in heaven, raising, 'with').
These categories are the pillars of their scriptural evidence.
3
Examine the argument for clarity
Read the final part where they claim these verses are muhkama. Their two criteria: abundance and lack of reinterpretation by early generations.
This argument is based on the assumption that silence implies acceptance of literalism.
4
Ask: Why does the claim of early silence matter?
Consider why the anthropomorphists think that if these verses were ambiguous, the Companions would have interpreted them. This is a key point that Razi will later address.
The argument hinges on the absence of transmitted interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: الآيات المشتملة على لفظ المروح إليه والصعود. قال تعالى: {تعرج الملائكة والروح إليه}، وقال: {إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب}. الخامس: الآيات المشتملة على لفظ الإنزال والتنزيل. قالوا: وهي كثيرة تزيد على المائتين في حق القرآن المبين، والروح والملائكة المقربين والتوراة والإنجيل. السادس: الآيات المقرونة بحرف إلى، مع أنها لانتهاء الغاية منها: قوله تعالى: {إلى ربها ناظرة}، وذلك يقتضى انتهاء النظر إليه، وقوله: {ثم إلى ربكم ترجعون}، وقوله: {وإلى المصير}، وقوله: {ارجعي إلى ربك}. السابع: قوله تعالى: {كلا إنهم عن ربهم يومئذ لمحجوبون}، والحجاب إنما يصح في حق من يكون جسما في جهة حتى يصير محجوبا بسبب شيء آخر. الثامن: الآيات الدالة على أنه في السماء. قال: {أم أمنتم من في السماء}، وقال: {قل لا يعلم من في السموات والأرض الغيب إلا الله}. التاسع: الآيات المشتملة على الرفع إليه. قال تعالى في حق عيسى عليه السلام: {إلى متوفيك ورافعك إلى}، وقوله: {بل رفعه الله إليه}. العاشر: الآيات المشتملة على العندية، كقوله: {إن الذين عند ربك}، وقوله: {عند مليك مقتدر}، وقوله: {رب ابن لي عندك بيتا في الجنة}، وقوله: {الذين عند ربك}، وقوله: {ومن عنده لا يستكبرون عن عبادته}. فهذا بيان وجوه تمسكاتهم من القرآن في إثبات الجهة لله تعالى. قالوا والذي يدل على أنها محكمة غير متشابهة: أنها في غاية الكثرة، وقوة الدلالة. ولو كانت من المتشابهات لتكلم فيها أحد من الصحابة والتابعين وذكروا تأويلاتها. وحيث لم ينقل عن أحد ذلك، علمنا أنها محكمة لا متشابهة.
Fourth: Verses that include the expression of ascent and rising. He said: {The angels and the spirit ascend to Him}, and He said: {To Him ascends the good speech}. Fifth: Verses that include the expression of sending down and revelation. They said: They are many, exceeding two hundred, concerning the clear Qur'an, the spirit, the near angels, the Torah, and the Gospel. Sixth: Verses connected with the letter 'to' (ila), indicating that it connotes the end of the goal: His saying: {Looking at their Lord}—this requires the end of looking to Him—and His saying: {Then to your Lord you will be returned}, and His saying: {And to the destination}, and His saying: {Return to your Lord}. Seventh: His saying: {No! Indeed, they are on that day veiled from their Lord}. The veil is only valid for one who is a body in a direction, such that he becomes veiled by something else. Eighth: Verses indicating that He is in heaven. He said: {Are you secure that He who is in heaven?} and He said: {Say, none in the heavens and earth knows the unseen except Allah}. Ninth: Verses that include raising to Him. He said concerning Jesus: {I will take you and raise you to Me}, and His saying: {Rather, Allah raised him to Him}. Tenth: Verses that include 'withness' (indiyya), such as His saying: {Indeed, those who are with your Lord}, and His saying: {With the Sovereign, the Omnipotent}, and His saying: {Lord, build for me with You a house in Paradise}, and His saying: {Those who are with your Lord}, and His saying: {And those with Him are not arrogant to worship Him}. This is an exposition of their arguments from the Qur'an for affirming direction (jiha) for Allah. They said, and what indicates that these verses are clear (muhkama) not ambiguous (mutashabiha): they are extremely numerous and strong in indication. If they were among the ambiguous, some Companion or Successor would have discussed them and mentioned their interpretations. Since none of that is transmitted, we know that they are clear, not ambiguous.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the anthropomorphists' classification of Quranic verses they use to argue that Allah has a direction (jiha) and a body. Imam Razi lists seven categories (the fourth through tenth) as part of a larger enumeration. Each category groups verses by a common theme: (4) verses mentioning ascent, (5) verses mentioning sending down, (6) verses with the preposition 'to' indicating an endpoint, (7) the verse on veiling, which implies a body, (8) verses saying 'He who is in heaven', (9) verses on raising, and (10) verses using 'with' (inda). After the list, the passage reports their argument that these verses are muhkamat (clear, unambiguous) because they are abundant and strongly indicative. They further claim that if these verses were mutashabihat (ambiguous), the early Companions and Successors would have discussed their interpretations, but since no such discussion is reported, the verses must be taken literally. This is a key proof for anthropomorphists, which Imam Razi will later refute.

Key Terms
جهة
محكمة
متشابهة
تأويل
عندية
حجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that listing these verses endorses the anthropomorphic interpretation.
Correction: Imam Razi is presenting them as claims to be refuted, not affirming them.

Checkpoint
According to the anthropomorphists, what indicates that the verses they cite are clear (muhkama) not ambiguous (mutashabiha)?
A) They are all from the same surah.
B) They are extremely numerous and strongly indicative, and early scholars did not reinterpret them.
C) They all contain the word 'direction'.
D) They are contradictory to philosophical proofs.

Key Takeaway
The anthropomorphists rely on the abundance of direction-related verses and the absence of early interpretations to argue that these verses are clear and literal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about Allah being 'in heaven' or 'above', do you immediately assume a literal direction? How does the anthropomorphists' argument from the abundance of such verses affect your confidence in that assumption?
?
If you were to defend the belief that Allah is not in a direction, how would you answer the claim that the early Muslims never reinterpreted these verses? What does that silence mean for your own approach to understanding Allah's attributes?
?
Can you identify a theological position you hold that relies on a similar argument from silence or abundance? Are you willing to re-examine that position in light of the possibility that silence might indicate tafwid rather than literalism?

The Tenth Category: Verses with 'Inda
Listing verses with 'inda and arguing they are unambiguous

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the anthropomorphist argument that Quranic verses using the word 'inda are clear evidence for Allah having a direction, and why they claim these verses are unambiguous.

Core Idea
The word 'inda is used to argue Allah has a spatial direction.

In this lesson, we examine a key proof used by those who affirm a physical direction for Allah. They point to several Quranic verses containing the word 'inda (with) and argue that these verses are clear and decisive, not ambiguous. The argument rests on two observations: the large number of such verses and the absence of any interpretation from the early generations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Get a sense of the examples given and the argument structure.
The passage lists five Quranic verses as evidence.
2
Identify the examples of 'inda
Notice each instance of 'inda in the quoted verses. Consider how the context might imply a spatial or locative meaning.
The verses are about being 'with' Allah or 'with' the Sovereign.
3
Understand the argument for clarity
The proponents claim these verses are muhkamat (clear) due to their abundance and strong indication. They argue that if they were ambiguous, early Muslims would have provided interpretations, but none are reported.
This is an argument from silence.
4
Reflect on the assumption
Consider whether the absence of interpretation necessarily proves clarity. Think about alternative possibilities the author might raise later.
This will help you prepare for the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر: الآيات المشتملة على العندية، كقوله: {إن الذين عند ربك}، وقوله: {عند مليك مقتدر}، وقوله: {رب ابن لي عندك بيتا في الجنة}، وقوله: {فالذين عند ربك}، وقوله: {ومن عنده لا يستكبرون عن عبادته}. فهذا بيان وجوه تمسكاتهم من القرآن في إثبات الجهة لله تعالى. قالوا: والذي يدل على أنها محكمة غير متشابهة: أنها في غاية الكثرة، وقوة الدلالة. ولو كانت من المتشابهات، لتكلم فيها أحد من الصحابة والتابعين وذكروا تأويلاتها. وحيث لم ينقل عن أحد ذلك، علمنا أنها محكمة لا متشابهة.
The Tenth: The verses that include the word 'inda (with), such as His saying: 'Indeed, those who are with your Lord' and His saying: 'With a Sovereign All-Powerful' and His saying: 'Lord, build for me a house with You in Paradise' and His saying: 'So those who are with your Lord' and His saying: 'And those who are with Him are not arrogant to worship Him.' This is an exposition of the aspects of their argumentation from the Quran in affirming direction for Allah Almighty. They said: And what indicates that they are clear (muhkamat) and not ambiguous (mutashabihat) is their great number and strength of indication. If they were from the ambiguous verses, some of the Companions or Followers would have spoken about them and mentioned their interpretations. And since none of that was transmitted from anyone, we know that they are clear, not ambiguous.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument used by those who affirm a direction for Allah (the anthropomorphists). The author begins by listing the tenth category of verses: those containing the word 'inda (عند). He gives five examples from the Quran. The arguer claims that these verses indicate that Allah has a direction because 'with' implies spatial relationship. Then he argues that these verses are muhkamat (clear and decisive) not mutashabihat (ambiguous), citing two reasons: their great number and the strength of their apparent meaning. If they were ambiguous, the early generations would have offered interpretations, but no such interpretations are reported. Therefore, they are clear proof that Allah is in a direction. The author is reporting this as the opponent's argument, which he will later refute.

Key Terms
العندية
المحكم
المتشابه
التأويل
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'inda always implies physical proximity or direction.
Correction: In Arabic, 'inda can also denote honor, status, or proximity in rank, not necessarily spatial location. The Ash'ari response is that these verses are to be interpreted metaphorically.

Checkpoint
How do the proponents of direction argue that the verses containing 'inda are clear (muhkamat) and not ambiguous?
A) Because the Prophet interpreted them.
B) Because they are few in number.
C) Because no Companion or Follower gave alternative interpretations.
D) Because they are all from Meccan suras.

Key Takeaway
The opponents use the frequency of 'inda verses and the silence of early Muslims to argue that these verses are clear proof for Allah having a direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'those with your Lord', do you automatically imagine Allah in a specific direction? What does that reveal about your natural inclination? Are you willing to discipline your mind to accept a more abstract but correct understanding of His transcendence, even if it feels less immediate?
?
You have just read an argument that uses the silence of the early Muslims to justify a literal interpretation. Is there any aspect of your faith where you rely on what you assume the early generations would have done, without checking the actual sources? What would it cost you to verify that assumption?
?
The opponents of the Ash'ari position prefer the apparent meaning of these verses because it feels safer and more tangible. Do you have any beliefs that you hold onto because they feel comfortable, even though you suspect deeper scholarship might challenge them? Will you deliberately seek out that challenge, or remain in your comfort?

The Bedouin's Request and the Prophet's Gesture
Reporting a hadith with a gesture indicating Allah's location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith used by proponents of direction for Allah, including the Prophet's rebuke and the significance of his gesture.

Core Idea
The Prophet's gesture affirms Allah's transcendence, but its interpretation is debated.

This hadith from Abu Dawud is often cited in discussions about Allah's location and the permissibility of seeking intercession. The bedouin's request to 'seek intercession with Allah through you' prompts a strong reaction from the Prophet, who emphasizes Allah's majesty and then indicates with his hand that Allah is above His Throne. The physical gesture has been interpreted both literally and metaphorically within Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hadith
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the chain of transmission and the key phrases used by the bedouin and the Prophet.
The hadith is narrated by Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn Mut'im from his father and grandfather.
2
Understand the Prophet's Rebuke
Observe how the Prophet glorifies Allah repeatedly and then rebukes the bedouin for saying 'we seek intercession with Allah through you'. The Prophet clarifies that Allah is too great to be used as an intercessor against anyone.
The Prophet's glorification continues until it is visible on his companions' faces.
3
Analyze the Gesture
The Prophet then says 'He is above His heavens on His Throne' and gestures with his hand like a dome. Reflect on what this gesture might indicate about Allah's relationship to the Throne and the heavens.
Some interpret this as affirming a physical direction, while Ash'aris see it as a gesture to help the bedouin understand transcendence.
4
Consider Theological Implications
Think about how this hadith is used in kalam arguments about divine attributes and direction. The additional note from Abu al-Azhar compares the creaking of a saddle with a rider, suggesting a physical contact above.
This hadith is key evidence for those who affirm a direction (jiha) for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخبر الأول: ما رواه أبو داود في باب الرد على الجهمية والمعتزلة، عن حسن بن محمد بن مطعم، عن أبيه، عن جده. قال: جاء أعرابي إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: يا رسول الله، هلكت الأنفس، وجاع العيال، وهلكت الأموال. فاستسق لنا ربك، فإننا نستشفع بالله عليك وبك على الله. فقال عليه السلام: سبحان الله، سبحان الله، فما زال يسبح حتى عرف ذلك في وجوه أصحابه. ثم قال: ويحك، أما تدري أن الله شأنه أعظم من ذلك؟ إنه لا يستشفع به على أحد. إنه لفوق سمواته على عرشه، وأنه عليه هكذا، وأشار بيده مثل القبة. وأشار أبو الأزهر أيضا: يأط به أطيط الرحل بالراكب.
The first report: What Abu Dawud narrated in the chapter on refuting the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila, from Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn Mut'im, from his father, from his grandfather. He said: A bedouin came to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and said: O Messenger of Allah, the souls have perished, the children are hungry, and the wealth has been destroyed. So ask your Lord to send us rain, for we seek intercession with Allah through you and through you to Allah. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Glory be to Allah, glory be to Allah,' and he continued glorifying until that was apparent in the faces of his companions. Then he said: 'Woe to you, do you not know that Allah, His majesty is greater than that? He is not sought as intercessor against anyone. He is above His heavens on His Throne, and He is upon it like this,' and he gestured with his hand like a dome. And Abu al-Azhar also indicated: 'There is a creaking of the saddle with the rider.'
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the first hadith evidence used by the proponents of direction. The hadith is from Abu Dawud. A bedouin asks the Prophet to pray for rain, saying 'we seek intercession with Allah through you and through you to Allah.' The Prophet rebukes him for this, glorifying Allah repeatedly, because Allah is too great to be used as an intercessor against someone. The Prophet then says that Allah is above His heavens on His Throne, and physically gestures with his hand to indicate that Allah is above, like a dome. The additional note from Abu al-Azhar compares it to the creaking of a saddle with a rider, suggesting a physical contact above. The author reports this as proof that the Prophet affirmed a direction for Allah.

Key Terms
الاستشفاع
العرش
السماء
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The gesture proves that Allah is literally in a specific spatial location above the Throne.
Correction: Ash'ari theologians interpret the gesture as a metaphor or as a gesture adapted to the bedouin's understanding, not as a literal description of Allah's location.

Checkpoint
What did the Prophet gesture with his hand to indicate?
A) The height of the sky.
B) The shape of the Throne.
C) That Allah is above like a dome.
D) The size of the sun.

Key Takeaway
This hadith from Abu Dawud shows the Prophet affirming that Allah is above the Throne with a hand gesture, but the interpretation of this gesture remains a key point of theological debate between literalists and metaphorical interpreters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What specific action will you take today to remind yourself that Allah's greatness cannot be used as a tool for personal gain?
?
The Prophet gestured to illustrate Allah's 'above-ness' to a simple bedouin. Do you reduce Allah to a limited concept in your mind, imagining Him in a way that suits your comfort? How can you cultivate a sense of awe for Allah's true transcendence without trying to confine Him to your own mental image?

The Slave Girl Hadith and Divine Location
Reporting the hadith and the argument for divine location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith of the slave girl, the apparent proof used by literalists, and the Ash'ari interpretation that preserves Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
The Prophet's approval of 'in heaven' does not imply a physical location for Allah.

The hadith of the slave girl is often cited by those who claim that Allah is physically in the sky. But how do scholars of Ahl al-Sunna interpret this? In this lesson, we examine the narration and the reasoning behind the Ash'ari understanding that Allah is above His creation without being contained in a place.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic and literal English translation carefully. Notice the sequence: question about freeing a slave, the Prophet's questions, the slave girl's answers, and his verdict.
Pay attention to the phrase 'fi al-sama'' (in heaven).
2
Examine the literalist proof
Understand why some argue that the Prophet's acceptance of her answer proves that Allah is literally in the sky. Identify their reasoning.
They take 'in heaven' as a spatial location.
3
Learn the Ash'ari interpretation
Contrast with the Ash'ari response: 'in heaven' means highness of rank and authority, not physical containment. The Prophet tested her belief, not His precise location.
This preserves tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخبر الثالث: ما أخرجه في الصحيح عن عمر بن الحكم، أنه قال: كنت عند النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقلت: يا رسول الله، إن لي جارية كانت ترعى غنما، فجئتها ففقدت شاة، فسألتها فقالت: أكلها الذئب، فأسفت عليها فلطمت وجهها وعلي رقبة، أفأعتقها؟ فقال لها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: أين الله؟ فقالت: في السماء. قال: من أنا؟ قالت: أنت رسول الله. فقال عليه السلام: اعتقها فإنها مؤمنة. قالوا: وهذا يدل على التصريح من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بأن الله في السماء.
The third report: What was narrated in the Sahih from 'Umar ibn al-Hakam, that he said: I was with the Prophet, peace be upon him, so I said: O Messenger of Allah, I have a female slave who was tending sheep, then I came to her and missed a sheep. I asked her, and she said: The wolf ate it. So I became angry and slapped her face, and I have a neck (to free). Should I free her? The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said to her: 'Where is Allah?' She said: 'In heaven.' He said: 'Who am I?' She said: 'You are the Messenger of Allah.' He said: 'Free her, for she is a believer.' They said: This indicates the explicit statement from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, that Allah is in heaven.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This is a well-known hadith used by those who affirm direction. The Prophet asks a slave girl 'Where is Allah?' and she responds 'In heaven' (في السماء). He then asks who he is, and she correctly identifies him as the Messenger of Allah. The Prophet then declares her a believer and instructs her owner to free her. The proponents argue that the Prophet's acceptance of her answer proves that Allah is literally in the sky, and that this is part of essential belief. The author records this as part of the opponent's evidence.

Key Terms
السماء
الإيمان
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this hadith proves Allah is physically in the sky, like a body occupying space.
Correction: Ash'aris interpret 'in heaven' as meaning that Allah is above the heavens in a manner befitting His majesty, not spatially contained. The Prophet's question was about the slave girl's recognition of Allah's supremacy, not a literal location.

Checkpoint
What was the slave girl's response when the Prophet asked her 'Where is Allah?'?
A) Above the Throne.
B) In the heavens.
C) Everywhere.
D) She was silent.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of the slave girl, when properly understood, does not support the claim that Allah is physically located in the sky; rather, it affirms His transcendent rank above creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you instinctively picture Him in a particular direction or place? How does that mental image align with the Ash'ari teaching that He is beyond spatial confinement? Are you ready to train your heart to think of Him without any physical association, even when it feels less concrete?
?
The slave girl answered 'in heaven' and was deemed a believer. If the Prophet asked you 'Where is Allah?' today, what would your answer be based on—a memorized phrase or a deeply held conviction about His transcendence? What might your honest answer reveal about your own 'aqeedah?
?
This hadith is often used to argue for a literal location for Allah. Have you ever felt a tension between such apparent texts and the clear principle of tanzih? Will you commit to seeking the knowledge needed to reconcile them—even if it means challenging your initial understanding?

The Rational Argument for Direction: Two Existents
Summarizing the rational proof for direction based on necessary knowledge of two existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that any two distinct existents must be either interpenetrating or separate by a direction.

Core Idea
Rational necessity demands that two distinct things occupy either the same space or different directions.

Before we examine the Ash'ari response, we first encounter the opponents' foundational rational claim. They assert that any two existing things must relate spatially in one of two ways. This passage summarizes their argument and notes that a comprehensive answer has already been given.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Read the Arabic sentence carefully. It states a 'necessary knowledge' (بالضرورة) about any two existents.
Notice the absolute nature of the claim: it applies to all existents.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The claim lists two possibilities: either one is 'حالا في الآخر' (interpenetrating/inhering in the other) or 'مباينا عنه بجهة' (separate by a direction).
This is meant to be exhaustive: there is no third possibility.
3
Understand the implication
If Allah and creation are distinct, then according to this argument, they must be separate by a direction. The author notes that a response has already been provided earlier.
This sets up the need for a refutation, which is not in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعقول: فقد تقدم من قولهم: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما حالا في الآخر أو مباينا عنه بجهة من الجهات. وتقدم الاستقصاء في الجواب عنها. وبالله التوفيق. وأما الوجوه المركبة من السمع والعقل فوجهان: الأول: قصة المعراج، تدل على أن المعبود مختص بجهة فوق. وربما تمسكوا في هذا المقام بقوله: {ثم دنى فتدلى، فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى}.
As for the rational proof: It has already preceded from their statement: We know by necessity that any two existents must be either one interpenetrating the other or separate from the other in some direction. And a comprehensive answer to them has already preceded. And with Allah is success. As for the combined proofs from revelation and reason, they are two: First: The story of the Mi'raj (Ascension) indicates that the object of worship is specific to a direction above. And perhaps they adduce in this place His saying: {Then he drew near and descended, and was at a distance of two bow-lengths or nearer}.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents two things. First, a summary of the rational argument used by the opponents: they claim that any two existing things must either occupy the same space (interpenetrate) or be separated by a direction. Since Allah and creation are distinct, they must be in separate directions. The author notes that a comprehensive answer to this has already been given earlier in the book. They also cite the Quranic verse about the Prophet's nearness to Allah during the Mi'raj: 'Then he drew near and descended, and was at a distance of two bow-lengths or nearer' (al-Najm 8-9). Key Takeaway
The rational argument for direction is based on the claim that two existents necessarily relate either by interpenetration or directional separation.

The Combined Proof from the Night Journey (Mi'raj)
Presenting the first combined proof from reason and revelation: the Mi'raj narrative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of the Mi'raj and the Quranic verse are used as combined evidence to argue that Allah is in a direction above.

Core Idea
The Mi'raj combines reason and revelation to argue that Allah is above.

After noting the rational argument, the author presents a second type of proof: one that combines both revelation and reason. This proof draws on the well-known story of the Prophet's Night Journey to heaven (Mi'raj) and a Quranic verse describing his nearness to Allah. Together, these are used to support the claim that Allah is specifically in the direction of 'above'.

Guided Path
1
Read the combined proof
Read the Arabic: 'وأما الوجوه المركبة من السمع والعقل فوجهان: الأول: قصة المعراج...' Notice it says 'two' (وجهان) but only mentions the first. The second is not in this passage.
The passage only gives one of the two combined proofs.
2
Understand the Mi'raj argument
The Mi'raj story is used as evidence that Allah is 'مختص بجهة فوق' (specific to the direction above). The reasoning: the Prophet ascended to meet his Lord, implying Allah is above.
This is a scriptural proof (سمع) combined with rational reasoning (عقل).
3
Examine the Quranic verse
They cite the verse 'ثم دنى فتدلى، فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى' (al-Najm 8-9) to further support the idea of nearness. This verse is about the Prophet's closeness to Allah during the Mi'raj.
The verse is used to show that nearness in direction is possible.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari response
Ash'aris typically interpret this as a miracle and not as literal spatial nearness. The direction argument is rejected.
This connects to the common misunderstanding.

Two Combined Arguments for Divine Direction
Imam Razi enumerates two combined arguments for divine direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two combined rational and revealed arguments used by anthropomorphists to assert that Allah is in a direction, and why these arguments are addressed in kalam.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists mix scripture and reason to claim Allah is in a direction.

Imam Razi now addresses arguments that combine revelation and rationality, used by those who claim Allah is confined to a direction. After dismissing the purely rational argument as already refuted, he presents two scripturally-based arguments: the Mi'raj story and Pharaoh's request for a tower. Understanding these helps clarify why the mainstream tradition refutes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Imam Razi first mentions that the purely rational argument has been dealt with, then introduces two combined arguments.
The passage is concise; each argument is presented in a few lines.
2
Understand the first argument from the Mi'raj
Focus on how the opponents use the verse 'Then He drew near and descended' to argue that drawing near implies physical direction, thus Allah is above.
The key terms are 'دنو' (drawing near) and 'جهة' (direction).
3
Understand the second argument from Pharaoh's tower
Notice how Pharaoh's attempt to build a tower to reach the god of Moses, and Moses' disapproval, is interpreted by opponents as proof that Allah is in the heaven.
This argument relies on the assumption that Moses rebuked Pharaoh for denying Allah's location.
4
Reflect on the role of these arguments
Consider why Imam Razi includes these arguments: to show the faulty reasoning of anthropomorphists, which will be refuted in subsequent lessons.
These arguments are not endorsed but presented for critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعقول : فقد تقدم من قولهم : إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن كل موجودين ، فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما حالا في الآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بجهة من الجهات . وتقدم الاستقصاء في الجواب عنها . وبالله التوفيق . وأما الوجوه المركبة من السمع والعقل فوجهان : الأول : قصة المعراج . تدل على أن المعبود مختص بجهة فوق . وربما تمسكوا في هذا المقام بقوله : « ثم دنى فتدلى ، فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى » وهذا يدل على أن ذلك الدنو بالجهة . ثم قال : « فأوحى إلى عبده ما أوحى » وهذا يدل على أن ذلك الدنو إنما كان من الله تعالى . وهذا يدل على أنه مختص بجهة فوق . الثاني : تمسكوا بقول فرعون : « يا هامان ابن لي صرحا ، لعلي أبلغ الأسباب . أسباب السموات . فأطلع إلى إله موسى » ثم إن موسى عليه السلام أنكر عليه هذا الكلام . فدل ذلك : على أن الإله في السماء . فهذه جملة ما يتمسكون به في هذا الباب .
As for the rational: it has preceded from their statement: we know by necessity that any two existents must be either one subsisting in the other, or separate from it by some direction. And the detailed refutation of it has preceded. And with Allah is success. As for the combined rational and revealed proofs, there are two: The first: the story of the Mi'raj. It indicates that the worshipped One is specific to the direction of above. And perhaps they cling in this place to His saying: 'Then He drew near and descended, until he was two bow-lengths away or nearer.' This indicates that that drawing near was by direction. Then He said: 'Then He revealed to His servant what He revealed.' This indicates that that drawing near was from Allah Almighty. And this indicates that He is specific to the direction of above. The second: They cling to the saying of Pharaoh: 'O Haman, build me a tower, that I might reach the causes. The causes of the heavens, and look at the god of Moses.' Then Moses (peace be upon him) disapproved of this statement. So that indicates that the god is in the heaven. So this is the totality of what they cling to in this chapter.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents two combined arguments (al-wujuh al-murakkaba min al-sam' wa al-'aql) used by the anthropomorphists. First, Imam Razi notes that the purely rational argument (al-ma'qul) – that any two existents must occupy a relationship of either inherence (hulul) or separation (mubayana) – has already been mentioned and refuted earlier. He then turns to two arguments that mix revelation and reason. The first is the story of the Mi'raj (ascension), where the Prophet drew near to Allah. The opponents argue that the phrase 'he drew near' implies physical proximity in direction (jiha), and that the revelation that followed shows the drawing near was to Allah, thus Allah is in a direction. The second is Pharaoh's request for a tower to ascend and see the god of Moses. Since Moses disapproved of this attempt, the opponents infer that Allah is indeed in the heavens, accessible from above. The passage type is enumeration and presentation of arguments.

Key Terms

دنو
المعراج
قاب قوسين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that scriptural references to drawing near or ascending imply Allah is physically located in a direction.
Correction: Mainstream Islamic theology interprets such passages metaphorically, affirming that Allah is transcendent and not confined to any direction.

Key Takeaway
The two combined arguments rely on interpreting scriptural terms literally to imply that Allah is in a direction.

Imam Razi's Two Refutations of Anthropomorphic Proofs
Imam Razi enumerates two types of refutation for anthropomorphic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's two methodological responses to proofs that appear to attribute direction to Allah, and how he uses the opponents' own principles to refute them.

Core Idea
Consistency demands: if limbs are figurative, so is direction.

In this passage, Imam Razi lays out his overall strategy for answering all the anthropomorphic proofs the opponents have presented. He does not respond to each one individually but instead offers two general answers. The first is a powerful argument from consistency: the Karamites already accept that verses about Allah's hands and face must be interpreted metaphorically because rational proofs show they are impossible. The same logic applies to direction. The second answer is a contingency: if the opponents doubt the rational proofs, then the discussion moves to establishing those proofs themselves.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Imam Razi has been presenting and refuting anthropomorphic proofs. Here he summarizes his method in two types of answers.
Pay attention to the opponents mentioned: the Karamites.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Note the two types of answers.
The passage begins with 'واعلم: أن لنا في الجواب عن هذه الكلمات: نوعان من الجواب'.
3
Analyze the first type
Identify the argument of consistency (ilzam). The Karamites accept ta'wil for limbs, so they must also accept it for direction.
This is a decisive refutation if they accept rational proofs as decisive.
4
Analyze the second type
Imam Razi considers the possibility that the opponents deny the decisiveness of rational proofs. In that case, the discussion shifts to proving the proofs themselves.
This shows Imam Razi's thoroughness and willingness to engage further.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why Imam Razi presents these two types as a comprehensive answer covering all cases.
This reflects the Ahl al-Sunnah approach of using rational certainty to interpret ambiguous texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن لنا في الجواب عن هذه الكلمات : نوعان من الجواب : النوع الأول : أن نقول للكرامية : أنتم ساعدتمونا على أن ظواهر القرآن ، وإن دلت على إثبات الأعضاء والجوارح لله تعالى ، فإنه يجب القطع بنفيها عن الله تعالى ، والجزم بأنه منزه عنها . وما ذاك إلا أنه لما قامت الدلائل القطعية على استحالة الأعضاء والجوارح على الله تعالى ، وجب القطع بتنزيه الله عنها ، والجزم بأن مراد الله تعالى من تلك الظواهر ، شيء آخر . فكذا في هذه المسألة : نحن ذكرنا الدلائل العقلية القاطعة في أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يكون مختصا بالمكان والجهة والحيز . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب القطع بأن مراد الله تعالى من هذه الظواهر التي تمسكتم بها ، شيء آخر سوى إثبات الجهة لله تعالى . وهذا إلزام قاطع ، وكلام قوي . إلا أن نقول : إن تلك الدلائل العقلية التي تمسكتم بها ، ليست قطعية . بل هي محتملة . فنحن إذا يجب علينا أن نتكلم معكم في تقرير تلك الدلائل .
Know that for us in answering these statements there are two types of answer: The first type: that we say to the Karamites: You have helped us that the apparent meanings of the Qur'an, even if they indicate proving limbs and organs for Allah Almighty, it is necessary to definitively negate them from Allah Almighty, and to be certain that He is transcendent above them. That is only because when decisive proofs have been established for the impossibility of limbs and organs for Allah Almighty, it is necessary to definitively declare Allah transcendent above them, and to be certain that the intention of Allah Almighty from those apparent meanings is something else. Similarly in this issue: we have mentioned the rational decisive proofs that it is impossible for the Exalted to be specific to place, direction, and space. If that is the case, then it is necessary to definitively say that the intention of Allah Almighty from these apparent meanings that you have clung to is something other than proving direction for Allah Almighty. This is a decisive entailment and a strong statement. Unless we say: Those rational proofs that you have clung to are not decisive; rather they are probable. Then we must discuss with you in establishing those proofs.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's two methodological answers (jawab) to the collection of proofs presented by the anthropomorphists. The first answer is a powerful argument from consistency (ilzam): the Karamites themselves accept that apparent verses about Allah having hands, face, etc. must be interpreted metaphorically because rational proofs demonstrate their impossibility. Similarly, rational proofs demonstrate that Allah cannot be in a direction, so the verses suggesting direction, such as 'above the Throne' or 'in the heaven,' must also be interpreted metaphorically. This is a decisive refutation (ilzam qati'). The second answer is a contingency: if the opponents object that these rational proofs are not decisive but merely probable, then Imam Razi concedes that the discussion must shift to evaluating those rational proofs themselves. The passage type is argument and refutation strategy.

Key Terms
الكرامية
ظواهر
الأعضاء والجوارح
قطعيات
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Imam Razi is denying the literal meaning of the Qur'an or rejecting hadith.
Correction: Imam Razi is not denying the revelation; he is affirming that rational certainty takes precedence when contradictory apparent meanings arise, and that ta'wil is a legitimate method to reconcile them.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Imam Razi's first type of answer?
A) The Karamites accept ta'wil for anthropomorphic verses about limbs/organs
B) The hadith about direction are weak
C) The Karamites are heretics
D) The verses about direction are unclear

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's two-type response shows that the opponents must either accept the logical consistency of interpreting direction verses metaphorically, or engage in establishing the rational proofs that necessitate that.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever applied a principle in one area of your life but refused to apply it in another, similar area? How does Imam Razi's consistency argument expose that hypocrisy, and what will you do today to align your practices with your stated beliefs?

?
Imam Razi prepares a second type of response in case the opponent questions the premises. Are you ready to defend the rational proofs for Allah's transcendence, or do you rely on weak arguments? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen your understanding of those proofs?

Refuting Literalist Interpretation of Istawa' on the Throne
Razi presents two contextual arguments against literal istawa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two arguments from the surrounding verses to reject that 'istawa' means physical settlement.

Core Idea
Context proves God is beyond place and direction.

Razi begins his detailed refutation of the Karamiyya's proof from the verses of istawa'. He argues that the verses before and after the istawa' verse in Surah Taha clearly indicate Allah's transcendence over space and direction. This lesson examines those two arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi structures his argument with 'First' and 'Second'.
2
Identify the two proofs
Razi presents two proofs: one from the preceding verse and one from the following verse. Find each and understand how they support his claim that 'istawa' cannot mean physical settlement.
The first proof relies on the phrase 'Creator of the earth and high heavens' implying transcendence. The second proof analyzes the meaning of 'heaven' as direction of above.
3
Reflect on the interpretative method
Consider how Razi uses the immediate context of a verse to interpret its meaning. This is a key principle of tafsir and kalam: the context limits literalist readings.
Razi does not reject the verse; he rejects a particular interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني : أن نتكلم عن كل واحد من هذه الوجوه على سبيل التفصيل : أما الذى تمسكوا به أولا . وهو الآيات الست الدالة على استواء الله تعالى على العرش. فنقول : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون مراد الله تعالى من ذلك الاستواء : هو الاستقرار على العرش . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن ما قبل هذه الآية، وهو قوله تعالى: « تنزيلا ممن خلق الأرض والسموات العلى ، قد بينا أن هذه الآية تدل على أنه تعالى غير مختص بشيء من الاحياز والجهات الثاني : إن ما بعد هذه الآية ، وهو قوله تعالى: « له ما في السموات وما في الأرض ، قد بينا : أن السماء هو الذى فيه سمو وفوقية ، فكل ما كان في جهة فوق ، فهو سماء . وإذا كان كذلك ، فقوله : له ما في السموات وما في الأرض ، يقتضى أن كل ما كان حاصلا فى جهة فوق ، كان في السماء . وإذا كان كذلك ، فقوله : له ما فى السموات وما في الأرض، يقتضى أن كل ما كان
The second type: that we speak about each one of these points in detail: As for what they clung to first, which is the six verses indicating God's istawa on the Throne. We say: it is not permissible that Allah's intent from that istawa is physical settlement on the Throne. And there are proofs for that: First: that before this verse, namely His statement: 'A revelation from the One who created the earth and the high heavens,' we have explained that this verse indicates that He is not confined to any of the spaces and directions. Second: that after this verse, namely His statement: 'To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth,' we have explained that 'heaven' is that which has elevation and height, so whatever is in a direction of above is heaven. And if so, then His statement: 'To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth' implies that everything that is in a direction of above is in heaven. And if so, then His statement: 'To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth' implies that everything that was
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage begins the second type of answer: a detailed verse-by-verse refutation. Razi takes the first scriptural evidence the Karamiyya use: the six verses that speak of Allah 'istawa' on the Throne. He immediately states that 'istawa' cannot mean physical sitting or settling. He offers two arguments. The first: the verse immediately preceding the istawa verse (Surah Taha, 20:4-5? or similar context) says 'a revelation from the One who created the earth and the high heavens'—this implies God's absolute transcendence and lack of confinement to any place or direction. The second: the verse after the istawa verse (e.g., 'to Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and the earth') is analyzed. Razi defines 'heaven' as anything with elevation and upwardness. Therefore, if everything in a direction of above is in heaven, then the statement 'to Him belongs whatever is in the heavens' would include everything above, but that does not mean God is located above; rather, it shows everything above belongs to Him, negating His being confined to a specific direction. The argument is cut off here.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Razi rejects the meaning of the istawa' verses entirely or that he denies the attribute of istawa'.
Correction: Razi only rejects the literalist interpretation of physical settlement (istiqrar). He affirms istawa' as an attribute but interprets it in a way befitting Allah's transcendence, as indicated by the surrounding verses.

B) The surrounding verses indicate God's transcendence over space and direction.
C) The Karamiyya accepted it.
D) There is no consensus.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the immediate context of the istawa' verses to prove that Allah's settling on the Throne cannot be physical, reinforcing divine transcendence.

Proof 1: The Preceding Verse Negates Spatial Confinement
Razi's first proof from the preceding verse of Q20:4.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse before the istiwa' verse (Q20:4) demonstrates that Allah is not confined to space or direction, refuting the literal interpretation.

Core Idea
The verse before 'istawa' shows Allah created all space, so He cannot be limited by it.

Imam Razi begins his detailed refutation by examining the immediate context of the istiwa' verse. The verse that comes before it speaks of Allah creating the earth and the high heavens, which implies His transcendence over all spatial dimensions. This lesson explores that first rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall meaning. Notice that Razi states his claim and then gives the first proof.
2
Identify the key claim
Understand that Razi uses the verse 'A revelation from Him who created the earth and the highest heavens' to argue that Allah is not particularized by any spaces or directions.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does creation of something imply the Creator is not limited by it? This reasoning is foundational to Islamic theology.
This connects to the broader principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني : أن نتكلم عن كل واحد من هذه الوجوه على سبيل التفصيل :
أما الذي تمسكوا به أولا . وهو الآيات السنة الدالة على استواء الله تعالى على العرش. فنقول : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون مراد الله تعالى من ذلك الاستواء : هو الاستقرار على العرش . ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول : إن ما قبل هذه الآية، وهو قوله تعالى: « تنزيلا ممن خلق الأرض والسموات العلى »، قد بينا أن هذه الآية تدل على أنه تعالى غير مختص بشيء من الأحياز والجهات .
الثاني : إن ما بعد هذه الآية ، وهو قوله تعالى: « له ما في السموات وما في الأرض »، قد بينا : أن السماء هو الذي فيه سمو وفوقية ، فكل ما كان في جهة فوق ، فهو سماء . وإذا كان كذلك ، فقوله : « له ما في السموات وما في الأرض »، يقتضي أن كل ما كان حاصلا في جهة فوق ، كان في السماء . وإذا كان كذلك ، فقوله : « له ما في السموات وما في الأرض »، يقتضي أن كل ما كان حاصلا في جهة فوق ، فهو ملك الله تعالى ومملوك له . فلو كان تعالى مختصا بجهة فوق ، لزم كونه مملوكا لنفسه من غير محل وهو محال . فثبت : أن ما قبل قوله « الرحمن على العرش استوى » وما بعده ينفي كونه سبحانه وتعالى مختصا بشيء من الأحياز والجهات . وإذا كان كذلك امتنع أن يكون المراد بقوله : « الرحمن على العرش استوى » : هو كونه مستقرا على العرش.
الثالث : أن ما قبل هذه الآية وما بعدها : مذكور لبيان كمال قدرة الله تعالى ، وغاية عظمته في الإلهية ، وكمال التصرف ، لأن قوله : « تنزيلا ممن خلق الأرض والسموات العلى » لا شك أن المفهوم منه : بيان كمال قدرة الله تعالى ، وكمال إلهيته . وقوله : « وله ما في السموات وما في الأرض وما بينهما وما تحت الثرى » بيان أيضا لكمال ملكه وإلهيته . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يكون قوله : ( الرحمن على العرش استوى ) كذلك وإلا لزم أن يكون ذلك كلاما أجنبيا عما قبله وعما بعده وذلك غير جائز . فأما إذا حملناه على كمال استيلائه على العرش ، الذي هو أعظم المخلوقات في الموجودات المحدثة ، كان ذلك موافقا لما قبل هذه الآية ولما بعدها . فكان هذا الوجه أولى .
الرابع: إن الجالس على العرش لابد وأن يكون الجزء الحاصل منه في يمين العرش ، غير الحاصل منه في يسار العرش : فيلزم كونه في نفسه مؤلفا ومركبا وذلك على الله تعالى محال .
The second type: that we speak about each of these aspects in detail:
As for that which they clung to first, and it is the six verses indicating Allah Almighty's istiwa' over the Throne. We say: It is not permissible that Allah Almighty intends by that istiwa' the meaning of physical settling (al-istiqrar) on the Throne. And this is indicated by aspects:
First: That which comes before this verse, which is His statement: 'A revelation from Him who created the earth and the highest heavens,' we have shown that this verse indicates that He, exalted, is not particularized by any of the spaces or directions.
Second: That which comes after this verse, which is His statement: 'To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth,' we have shown that the heaven is that which has elevation and highness, so everything that is in a direction of above is a heaven. And if that is so, then His statement: 'To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth' necessitates that everything occurring in a direction of above is in the heaven. And if that is so, then His statement: 'To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth' necessitates that everything occurring in a direction of above is the property of Allah Almighty and possessed by Him. So if He, exalted, were particularized by a direction of above, it would necessitate His being possessed by Himself without a locus, and that is impossible. So it is established: that what comes before His statement 'The Most Merciful established over the Throne' and what comes after negate His, glorified and exalted, being particularized by any of the spaces and directions. And if that is so, it is impossible that what is intended by His statement 'The Most Merciful established over the Throne' is His being settled upon the Throne.
Third: That which comes before this verse and after it is mentioned to demonstrate the perfection of Allah Almighty's power, the utmost of His greatness in divinity, and the perfection of disposition, because His statement: 'A revelation from Him who created the earth and the highest heavens' — there is no doubt that the understood meaning from it is the demonstration of the perfection of Allah's power and the perfection of His divinity. And His statement: 'And to Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is in the earth and whatever is between them and whatever is under the soil' is also a demonstration of the perfection of His dominion and His divinity. And if the matter is so, it is necessary that His statement: 'The Most Merciful established over the Throne' also be such, otherwise that speech would be foreign to what is before it and what is after it, and that is not permissible. As for when we interpret it as the perfection of His domination over the Throne, which is the greatest of creations among originated beings, that would be consistent with what is before this verse and what is after it. So this aspect is more appropriate.
Fourth: The one sitting on the Throne necessarily has the part of him that is on the right side of the Throne not the same as the part of him on the left side of the Throne; so it necessitates that he is in himself composed and composite, and that is impossible with respect to Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the second approach of Imam Razi: a detailed refutation of the Karramiyya's proof from the six verses about istiwa'. He states that the intended meaning cannot be physical settling (istiqrar) on the Throne, and provides four proofs.
1. The first proof: The verse preceding the istiwa' verse (Q20:4) indicates that Allah is not confined to spaces or directions. Since He created the earth and high heavens, He is not limited by them.
2. The second proof: The verse following the istiwa' verse (Q20:6) says 'To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and the earth.' Razi argues that 'heaven' refers to anything high, and everything in a high direction is in the heaven and belongs to Allah. If Allah were Himself in a high direction, He would be possessed by Himself (as part of His property), which is impossible. Therefore, the context negates direction.
3. The third proof: The surrounding verses speak of Allah's perfect power and divinity. The istiwa' verse must be interpreted in line with that theme. Interpreting istiwa' as domination (istila') over the Throne fits the context, while physical settling would be foreign to it.
4. The fourth proof: If someone sits on a throne, different parts of the body occupy different positions, implying composition and parts. This is impossible for Allah, who is not composed. Therefore, literal sitting is impossible. Each proof independently refutes the literal interpretation.

Key Terms
استواء
استقرار
أحياز
جهات

Checkpoint
In the first proof, what does Razi use to argue that Allah is not particularized by spaces?
A) The verse 'To Him belongs what is in the heavens and earth'
B) The verse 'A revelation from Him who created the earth and the highest heavens'
C) The verse 'The Most Merciful established over the Throne'
D) The verse 'And He is with you wherever you are'

Key Takeaway
The first rational proof shows that the preceding verse's emphasis on Allah's creation of all spaces directly negates any spatial confinement for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you recite or hear verses about Allah's throne, do you subconsciously imagine a physical object in a specific place? How does the truth that Allah created all space and direction challenge that mental image? What will you do to retrain your mind to think of His transcendence instead?
?
If you have ever believed that Allah 'sits' on the Throne in a literal sense, what is the source of that belief—cultural upbringing, a hadith, or your own assumption? Are you willing to reconsider it based on the clear evidence from the preceding verse? How would you respond if someone asked you to prove that Allah is not confined to space?
?
The verse 'A revelation from Him who created the earth and the highest heavens' is often read as mere background. But here it becomes a decisive proof. Have you ever overlooked a verse because it seemed ordinary? What might be required of you to always read the Quran with the awareness that every verse carries deep implications about Allah's nature?

Proof 2: The Following Verse and the Impossibility of Self-Possession
Razi's second proof from the following verse (Q20:6) about ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse after the istiwa' verse (Q20:6) leads to a logical impossibility if Allah were in a direction.

Core Idea
If Allah were in a high direction, He would be part of His own property, which is absurd.

Razi's second proof examines the verse that follows the istiwa' verse: 'To Him belongs whatever is in the heavens and the earth.' By defining 'heaven' as anything high, every high thing is Allah's property. If Allah Himself were in a high direction, He would be owned by Himself without a locus—a contradiction. This lesson unravels this logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key premise
Razi defines 'heaven' as anything that has elevation and highness. This is crucial for the argument.
2
Apply the premise
Everything in a high direction is in the heaven and thus belongs to Allah according to Q20:6.
3
Identify the contradiction
If Allah were in a high direction, He would be part of what belongs to Him, implying He is owned by Himself—an impossibility.
This is a logical reductio ad absurdum.
4
Conclude
Therefore, Allah cannot be in a direction, and literal istiwa' is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
