Razi's Defense of the Istila Interpretation
Razi presents four objections to interpreting istawa as istila and answers them, then gives two reasons for specifying the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's method of defending the interpretation of 'istawa' as 'istila' by redefining istila as perfect power without opponent and explaining the special status of the Throne.

Core Idea
Razi redefines istila to avoid anthropomorphism while affirming God's absolute power.

This lesson examines four objections to interpreting 'istawa' as 'istila' (domination) and Razi's decisive responses. He shows that the objections rely on a misunderstanding of 'istila' and then explains why the Throne is singled out. Pay attention to how Razi shifts the meaning of istila to preserve divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and answers
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how each objection is phrased and how Razi's single answer addresses all of them.
2
Analyze the redefinition of istila
Identify how Razi redefines 'istila' to answer the objections. What key phrase does he use? This is the core of his response.
Look for the phrase 'القدرة التامة الخالية عن المنازع والمعارض والمدافع'.
3
Understand the specification of the Throne
Read the two reasons given for singling out the Throne. The second reason from Al-Ghazali is more elaborate. Reflect on the analogy of the brain and the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا التأويل غير جائز . لوجوه : الأول : إن الاستيلاء عبارة عن صول الغلبة بعد العجز ، وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال الثانى : إنه إنما يقال : فلان استوى على كذا ، إذا كان له منازع ينازعه وذلك فى حق الله تعالى محال . الثالث : إنه إنما يقال فلان استولى على كذا : إذا كان المستولى عليه موجودا قبل ذلك وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال . لأن العرش إنما حدث بتكوينه وتخليقه . الرابع : إن الاستيلاء بهذا المعنى حاصل بالنسبة إلى كل المخلوقات. فلا يبقى لتخصيص العرش بالذكر فائدة . والجواب: إن مرادنا بالاستيلاء القدرة التامة الخالية عن المنازع والمعارض والمدافع . وعلى هذا التقدير، فقد زالت هذه المطاعن بأسرها . وأما تخصيص العرش بالذكر . ففيه وجهان : الأول : إنه أعظم المخلوقات ، فخص بالذكر لهذا السبب ، كما أنه خصه بالذكر في قوله : هو رب العرش العظيم لهذا المعنى . قال الشيخ الغزالي - رحمه الله . في كتاب إلجام العوام : «السبب في هذا التخصيص : هو أنه تعالى يتصرف في جميع العالم ، ويدير الأمر من السماء إلى الأرض ، بواسطة العرش فإنه تعالى لا يحدث صورة في العالم مالم يحدثها في العرش كما لا يحدث النقاش والكاتب صورة البناء على البياض ، مالم يحدثها في الدماغ ، بل لا يحدث صورة البناء في الخارج . مالم يحدث صورته في الدماغ ، بواسطة القلب والدماغ يدبر الروح أمر عالمه الذي هو يدبر فكذا بواسطة العرش يدبر الله أمر كل العالم»
If it is said: This interpretation is impermissible for reasons: First: Domination (istila') is an expression of the assault of overcoming after incapability, and that is impossible with respect to Allah, exalted. Second: One only says 'so-and-so took control (istawa) of such-and-such' if he has an opponent who contends with him, and that is impossible with respect to Allah. Third: One only says 'so-and-so took control (istawla) of such-and-such' if the thing taken control of existed before that, and that is impossible with respect to Allah, because the Throne only came into existence through His bringing it into being and creating it. Fourth: Domination in this sense applies to all creatures, so there remains no benefit in specifying the Throne. The answer: Our intention by 'domination' is perfect power free from any opponent, adversary, or defender. On this estimation, all these criticisms have been removed. As for specifying the Throne, there are two aspects: First: It is the greatest of created things, so it is specified for this reason, just as He specified it in His saying: 'He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' for this meaning. Al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, said in the book 'Iljam al-Awam': 'The reason for this specification is that He, exalted, acts in the entire world and directs the command from heaven to earth by means of the Throne. For He, exalted, does not bring about any form in the world unless He brings it about in the Throne, just as the painter and writer do not bring about the form of a building on a white surface unless they bring it about in the brain; rather, they do not bring about the form of a building externally unless they bring about its form in the brain. By means of the heart and brain, the soul manages the world that it manages. Similarly, by means of the Throne, Allah manages the matter of the entire world.'
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Razi presents four objections to interpreting 'istawa' as 'istila' (domination) and then answers them all. The objections are: (1) Domination implies prior incapability; (2) Domination requires an opponent; (3) Domination requires the thing dominated to pre-exist; (4) Domination applies to all creatures, so why specify the Throne? Razi's answer is clear: he redefines 'istila' not as overcoming after weakness or with an opponent, but as 'perfect power devoid of any opponent, adversary, or defender'. Once this meaning is adopted, all four objections fall away. Then he addresses the specificity of the Throne with two points. First, the Throne is the greatest creation, so it is singled out for honor, similar to the verse 'Lord of the Mighty Throne'. Second, he quotes Al-Ghazali's 'Iljam al-Awam', which gives a deeper reason: the Throne is the means through which Allah manages the entire world, analogous to how the brain manages the body via the heart. Just as the brain is the locus of management for the body, the Throne is the locus of divine management for the cosmos. This explanation shows that specifying the Throne is not arbitrary but meaningful.

Key Terms
استيلاء
استوى
العرش
القدرة التامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objections to the istila interpretation are insurmountable, or that istila necessarily implies anthropomorphism.
Correction: Razi shows that istila can be understood as perfect power without any opponent or prior incapability, so the objections are not valid.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the first objection that 'istila' implies prior incapability?
A) He agrees and says 'istawa' means something else.
B) He argues that the Throne existed before creation.
C) He redefines 'istila' as perfect power without any opponent or prior incapability.
D) He says the objection applies only to humans, not to God.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends the istila interpretation by redefining it as perfect power devoid of opposition, and explains the Throne's specification as the greatest creation and the means of divine cosmic management.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you truly believe that God’s power is 'perfect and free from any opponent'? Or do you sometimes act as if there are forces that can thwart His will? What changes in your reliance on Him if you internalize this lesson?
?
The Throne is the means by which Allah manages the world. Where do you tend to place your trust—in the means or in the One behind them? Are there areas where you are more attached to the cause than to the Cause of causes?
?
Razi defended an interpretation that might seem controversial. Are there beliefs you hold—concerning God’s attributes—that you are afraid to examine because they challenge your comfort? Would you be willing to let go of an anthropomorphic image of God for a more transcendent one, even if it feels less intimate?

Refuting Anthropomorphism: The Two Meanings of 'Fawq'
Razi refutes anthropomorphic interpretation by showing 'fawq' can mean superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses linguistic analysis to refute anthropomorphic interpretations of Quranic verses that mention 'fawq' (above).

Core Idea
The word 'fawq' can mean superiority in rank, not just physical direction.

In this passage, Imam Razi tackles a key argument of anthropomorphists who cite Quranic verses mentioning 'fawqiyya' (being above). He shows that the Arabic word 'fawq' is ambiguous and can denote superiority in rank and power, as proven by multiple Quranic examples. This linguistic insight is crucial for defending divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponents cling to verses that literally say 'above' to claim Allah has a physical direction. Read the passage to see Razi's summary of their position.
The opponent's argument is stated briefly at the beginning.
2
Razi's initial answer: two meanings of 'fawq'
Razi immediately points out that 'fawq' is used in Arabic for rank/power as well as direction. Identify the two meanings he mentions.
He uses the terms 'al-rutba wa al-qudra' (rank and power) and 'al-jiha' (direction).
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Razi provides three verses where 'fawq' clearly means superiority, and one where it means 'greater in a quality'. List these verses and note how they support his linguistic point.
The verse about the mosquito shows 'fawq' meaning 'greater in smallness'.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Razi asks: since the word is ambiguous, why do they insist on the directional meaning? Consider why this question is effective in refuting their argument.
The burden of proof is on the opponents to justify their specific interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الذي ذكروه ثانياً وهو التمسك بالآيات المشتملة على ذكر الفوقية. فجوابه : أن لفظ الفوق يستعمل في الرتبة والقدرة. فقد قال الله تعالى : ( وفوق كل ذي علم عليم ) ( وإنا فوقهم قادرون ) ( يد الله فوق أيديهم ) والمراد بالفوقية في هذه الآيات : الفوقية بالقهر والقدرة . ويستعمل في الفوق الذي بمعنى الجهة . قال تعالى : ( بعوضة فما فوقها ) أي أزيد منها في صفة الصغر والحقارة . وإذا كان لفظ الفوق محتملا للفوق فى الرتبة ، والفوق في الجهة ، فلم حملوه على الفوق في الجهة ؟
As for what they mentioned secondarily — namely, clinging to the verses that contain mention of 'fawqiyya' (superiority/above-ness). The answer to it is that the word 'al-fawq' is used for rank and power. For Allah most high said: 'and above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing' and 'and We are above them, dominant' and 'the hand of Allah is above their hands'. And what is meant by 'fawqiyya' in these verses is the superiority of subjugation and power. And it is also used for 'al-fawq' which means direction. He said most high: 'a mosquito and what is above it' meaning greater than it in the attribute of smallness and contempt. And since the word 'al-fawq' is possible for superiority in rank and for being above in direction, why did they interpret it as being above in direction?
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the opponents' second argument and Razi's initial response. The opponents have appealed to Quranic verses that mention 'fawqiyya' (being above). Razi answers by noting that the word 'fawq' has two possible meanings: superiority in rank and power, and direction/location. He provides Quranic examples where it clearly means superiority ('above every possessor of knowledge', 'We are above them', 'the hand of Allah is above their hands') and one example where it means direction or increase in a quality ('a mosquito and what is above it' meaning smaller). He then asks the rhetorical question: since the word is ambiguous between the two, why do they insist on the directional interpretation?

Key Terms
فوق
فوقية
رتبة
قدرة
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'fawq' always refers to physical direction or location.
Correction: Razi shows that 'fawq' has two meanings: superiority in rank/power and direction. In Quranic verses about Allah, it should be understood as superiority, not physical direction.

Checkpoint
What two meanings does Razi say the word 'fawq' can have?
A) Rank/power and direction
B) Above and below
C) Physical and metaphorical
D) Literal and figurative

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that the Arabic word 'fawq' can imply superiority in rank or power, not necessarily physical direction, thus refuting anthropomorphic interpretations of Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses like 'Yadullahi fawqa aydihim', do you catch yourself imagining Allah's hand physically above? What does Razi's argument expose about your assumptions? How will you train yourself to think of Allah's 'above-ness' as superiority from now on?
?
The anthropomorphists insisted on the literal direction because they lacked linguistic humility. In what area of your faith do you stubbornly cling to a surface-level understanding? Will you commit today to studying the deeper meanings of Allah's attributes?
?
If you had to explain to someone why 'fawq' in the Quran does not mean Allah is physically above, would your answer rely on Razi's reasoning? How can you internalize this argument so that it transforms how you worship—not because you picture Allah, but because you exalt Him in rank?

Three Proofs that 'Fawq' Means Power and Authority
Razi enumerates three proofs that 'fawq' means power and authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three textual and rational arguments from Razi demonstrating that 'fawq' in verses about Allah denotes superiority in power, not direction.

Core Idea
'Fawq' in the Quran means power, not position.

Imam Razi presents three compelling proofs to show that when the Quran says Allah is 'above' His servants, it means He has power over them, not that He occupies a physical location. Each argument appeals to logic, textual consistency, and the nature of divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Razi's three proofs unfold.
Note that the proofs are numbered as 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof uses the verse 'وهو القاهر فوق عباده' (He is the Subjugator over His servants). Razi argues that pairing 'fawq' with 'qahr' (subjugation) shows the superiority is one of power. He gives the analogy of a guard above a sultan in location but not in status.
This proof is linguistic and contextual.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof uses verses about Allah being 'with' (ma'iyya) His servants. Since 'ma'iyya' is understood metaphorically (knowledge, protection) by consensus, 'fawqiyya' should similarly be metaphorical.
This is an argument by analogy from the same scripture.
4
Understand the third proof
The third proof is rational: if 'fawq' meant physical direction, then the location itself would be more perfect than Allah. Razi anticipates the objection about power being an attribute and distinguishes that power is dependent on Allah while location is independent.
This proof uses philosophical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على أن المراد بلفظ الفوق ههنا : الفوق بالقدرة والمكنة وجوه : الأول : إنه قال : « وهو القاهر فوق عباده » والفوقية المقرونة بالقهر ، هى الفوقية بالقدرة والمكنة ، لا بمعنى الجهة . بدليل : أن الحارس قد يكون فوق السلطان فى الجهة ، ولا يقال إنه فوق السلطان. الثاني : إنه تعالى وصف نفسه بأنه مع عبيده فقال : « إن الله مع الذين اتقوا ، والذين هم محسنون » (وقال : « إن الله مع الصابرين ») وقال : « وهو معكم أينما كنتم » وقال : « ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد » وقال : « وإذا سألك عبادي عنى فإني قريب » وقال : « ما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم » وإذا جاز حمل المعية في هذه الآيات على المعية بمعنى العلم والحفظ والحراسة فلم لا يجوز حمل الفوقية فى الآيات التي ذكرتم على الفوقية بالقهر والقدرة والسلطنة ؟ الثالث : إن الفوقية الحاصلة بسبب الجهة ، ليست صفة المدح، لأن تلك الفوقية حاصلة للجهة والحيز بعينها وذاتها ، وحاصلة للمتمكن في ذلك الحيز. ولو كانت الفوقية بالجهة صفة مدح ، لزم أن الحيز بسبب تكون الجهة أفضل وأكمل من الله تعالى. ولا يقال : يلزمكم أن تقولوا : بأن القدرة أفضل وأكمل من الله تعالى . لأنا نقول : القدرة صفة القادر ، و ممتنعة الوجود بدونه ، بخلاف الحيز والجهة . فإنه غنى عن الممكن. فثبت : أن الكمال والفضيلة إنما يحصلان بسبب الفوقية ، بمعنى القدرة والسلطنة . فكان حمل الآية عليه أولى .
And what indicates that what is meant by the word 'al-fawq' here is the superiority of power and ability are aspects [proofs]: First: He said: 'And He is the Subjugator over His servants.' And 'fawqiyya' conjoined with subjugation is the superiority of power and ability, not in the sense of direction. As evidence: a guard may be above a sultan in direction, yet it is not said that he is above the sultan. Second: He most high described Himself as being 'with' His servants: He said: 'Indeed Allah is with those who fear Him and those who do good.' And He said: 'Indeed Allah is with the patient.' And He said: 'And He is with you wherever you are.' And He said: 'And We are closer to him than the jugular vein.' And He said: 'And when My servants ask you about Me, then I am near.' And He said: 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them.' And if it is permissible to interpret 'conjunction' (ma'iyya) in these verses as conjunction in the sense of knowledge, preservation, and protection, then why is it not permissible to interpret 'fawqiyya' in the verses you mentioned as superiority of subjugation, power, and authority? Third: The 'fawqiyya' that results from direction is not an attribute of praise, because that 'fawqiyya' belongs to the direction and location in itself and its essence, and belongs to the one situated in that location. And if 'fawqiyya' by direction were an attribute of praise, it would necessarily follow that the location, because of the direction, is better and more perfect than Allah most high. And let it not be said: 'It would follow that you say that power is better and more perfect than Allah most high.' For we say: Power is an attribute of the Powerful, and it is impossible in existence without Him, unlike location and direction, for it is independent of the contingent. So it is established that perfection and virtue only occur due to 'fawqiyya' in the sense of power and authority. Therefore, interpreting the verse that way is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (وجوه) demonstrating that the intended meaning of 'fawq' in the relevant verses is superiority in power, not direction. The first proof: The verse 'وهو القاهر فوق عباده' pairs 'fawqiyya' with 'qahr' (subjugation). This pairing shows that the superiority is one of power, not location. Razi supports this with an analogy: a guard may be physically above a sultan, but that does not make him superior to the sultan. The second proof: The Quran also uses 'ma'iyya' (with-ness) which opponents accept as metaphorical (knowledge, preservation). By analogy, 'fawqiyya' should also be interpreted metaphorically as power and authority. The third proof: Direction-based superiority is not a praiseworthy attribute, because the location itself would then be more perfect than God. Razi anticipates an objection: that the same could be said for power. He refutes it by noting that power is an attribute of God and cannot exist without Him, whereas location is independent of Him. Thus, only power-based superiority is genuinely praiseworthy.

Key Terms

قهر
قدرة
مكنة
معية
جهة
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogical argument from 'ma'iyya' claims that 'fawq' is exactly the same as 'ma'iyya'.
Correction: Razi is not claiming identity; he is arguing that if one can interpret 'ma'iyya' non-literally, then one can also interpret 'fawq' non-literally. The specific interpretation is different (power vs. knowledge).

Checkpoint
In the first proof, what analogy does Razi give to show that 'fawqiyya' with 'qahr' means power?
A) A guard above a sultan
B) A king above a slave
C) A father above a son
D) A mountain above a valley

Key Takeaway
Razi's three proofs collectively establish that the 'fawq' of Allah is one of power and authority, not physical elevation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'He is above His servants', do you catch yourself imagining a physical direction? How does Razi's third proof—that such a belief would make a location more perfect than Allah—challenge your own assumptions? What will you do to purify your understanding?
?

?
The proof from 'ma'iyya' shows consistency in how we interpret divine attributes. Are you consistent in trusting Allah's nearness (ma'iyya) during trials, or do you treat Him as physically remote? What specific reminder can you use this week to keep His 'withness' real to your heart?

Razi's Alternative Interpretation of 'From Above Them'
Razi proposes an alternative grammatical connection for the phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi reinterprets the Qur'anic phrase 'from above them' to defend divine transcendence, showing that it refers to the source of punishment, not Allah's location.

Core Idea
The phrase 'from above them' describes the direction of punishment, not God's location.

One common proof text cited by those who claim God is spatially above is the verse about angels fearing their Lord from above. However, Imam Razi offers an alternative grammatical and theological interpretation that upholds Allah's transcendence. In this lesson, we examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Razi introduces an 'other answer' to the verse.
2
Identify the grammatical shift
Razi says 'من فوقهم' is connected to the verb 'يخافون', not to 'ربهم'. This changes the meaning: 'they fear their Lord from above them' becomes 'they fear from above them their Lord'.
The grammatical term 'صلة' indicates attachment.
3
Understand the theological implication
The phrase 'from above them' now refers to the source of fear—punishment descending from above—rather than describing Allah's location. This preserves tanzih (transcendence).
Punishment is sent from above, but this does not imply Allah is above in a spatial sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله تعالى في صفة الملائكة : ( يخافون ربهم من فوقهم ) ففيه جواب آخر : وهو أنه يحتمل أن يكون قوله : « من فوقهم » صلة لقوله: يخافون ، أى يخافون من فوقهم ربهم . وذلك لأنهم يخافون نزول العذاب عليهم من جانب فوقهم.
As for His statement most high regarding the description of the angels: 'They fear their Lord from above them,' there is another answer concerning it: and it is that it is possible that His statement 'from above them' is related to His statement 'they fear', meaning they fear their Lord from above them. And that is because they fear the descent of punishment upon them from the direction above them.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific verse that opponents might use to argue for divine location: 'يخافون ربهم من فوقهم' (They fear their Lord from above them). Razi offers an alternative interpretation: the phrase 'من فوقهم' (from above them) is not a description of God's location, but rather it is connected to the verb 'يخافون' (they fear). The meaning is that they fear their Lord, and the fear comes from the direction above them, i.e., they fear punishment descending upon them from above. Thus, 'from above them' refers to the direction from which punishment comes, not where God is.

Key Terms
يخافون
من فوقهم
عذاب
نزول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'يخافون ربهم من فوقهم' proves that Allah is physically above the angels.
Correction: Razi shows that the phrase 'من فوقهم' is grammatically connected to the verb 'يخافون', referring to the source of fear (punishment from above), not to Allah's location.

Checkpoint

A) It describes God's location above them
B) It is an adverbial phrase connected to their fear of punishment from above
C) It means the angels are above God
D) It is a reference to the sky

Key Takeaway
Razi's interpretation of 'from above them' as modifying the fear rather than the Lord preserves divine transcendence by denying spatial direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Analogy of Ma'iyya and Fawqiyya
Razi draws an analogy between 'with' and 'above' to argue for metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the metaphorical interpretation of divine 'withness' (ma'iyya) as a precedent for interpreting divine 'aboveness' (fawqiyya) metaphorically.

Core Idea
If 'with' can be metaphorical, then 'above' can be too.

Imam Razi presents a powerful argument by analogy: the Quran uses the term 'ma'iyya' (with) for Allah in many verses, and scholars unanimously interpret it metaphorically as knowledge and protection, not spatial nearness. So why should 'fawqiyya' (above) be taken literally? This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice the list of Quranic verses Razi quotes to establish the usage of 'ma'iyya'.
2
Understand the analogy
Razi draws a parallel: just as 'ma'iyya' is interpreted metaphorically (as knowledge, preservation, protection), so should 'fawqiyya' be interpreted metaphorically (as dominance, power, authority). Reflect on why this is a strong refutation of literalism.
3
Identify the opponent's position
Think about who Razi is arguing against – those who insist on a literal, spatial meaning for 'Allah is above'. This argument shows inconsistency in their method.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this analogical reasoning protects divine transcendence (tanzih) while affirming Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إنه تعالى وصف نفسه بأنه مع عبيده فقال: «إن الله مع الذين اتقوا، والذين هم محسنون»، وقال: «إن الله مع الصابرين»، وقال: «وهو معكم أينما كنتم»، وقال: «ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد»، وقال: «وإذا سألك عبادي عني فإني قريب»، وقال: «ما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم»، وإذا جاز حمل المعية في هذه الآيات على المعية بمعنى العلم والحفظ والحراسة فلم لا يجوز حمل الفوقية في الآيات التي ذكرتم على الفوقية بالقهر والقدرة والسلطنة؟
The second: He, exalted, described Himself as being with His servants, saying: 'Indeed Allah is with those who fear Him and those who are doers of good.' And He said: 'Indeed Allah is with the patient.' And He said: 'And He is with you wherever you are.' And He said: 'And We are nearer to him than his jugular vein.' And He said: 'And when My servants ask you about Me, then indeed I am near.' And He said: 'There is no secret conversation of three except that He is their fourth.' So if it is permissible to interpret 'with' in these verses as 'with' in the sense of knowledge, preservation, and protection, then why is it not permissible to interpret 'above' in the verses you have mentioned as 'above' in the sense of dominance, power, and authority?
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of Imam Razi in refuting a literal, spatial interpretation of divine 'above-ness'. He employs an analogy: just as the Quranic verses describing Allah as 'with' (ma'iyya) His servants are universally interpreted metaphorically—meaning knowledge, preservation, and protection, not spatial nearness—so too should the verses describing Allah as 'above' (fawqiyya) be understood metaphorically as dominance and power. Razi lists several Quranic verses to establish that the attribute of 'ma'iyya' is clearly non-spatial, thereby setting a precedent for interpreting 'fawqiyya' in a similar figurative manner. This rebutts the opponent's claim that 'above' must be taken literally.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy denies any form of divine elevation; Allah is not truly above.
Correction: The analogy denies spatial elevation but affirms that Allah is above in majesty, power, and authority. It upholds tanzih while preserving the attribute of 'above-ness' in a manner befitting Allah.

Key Takeaway
Razi's analogical argument demonstrates that consistent interpretation of Quranic attributes requires understanding 'fawqiyya' metaphorically as divine dominion, not spatial location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This lesson shows that the Quran uses both 'with' and 'above' to draw you closer to Allah in a spiritual, not physical, way. Spatial Above-ness is Not Praiseworthy
Razi argues that spatial above-ness is not a praiseworthy attribute for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that spatial direction cannot be a praiseworthy attribute for Allah, and how he distinguishes between accidental properties and true perfections.

Core Idea
If spatial above-ness were a perfection, the space itself would be more perfect than Allah.

Imam Razi presents a logical argument to show that interpreting Allah's 'above-ness' in a spatial sense leads to an absurdity: it would make the location superior to Allah. He then addresses a possible objection about power being a perfection, clarifying the difference between essential and accidental attributes. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Argument
Read Razi's claim that directional above-ness is not praiseworthy because it belongs equally to the space and its occupant. If it were a perfection, the space would be more perfect than Allah.
Notice the conditional reasoning: if A, then B (absurd), therefore not-A.
2
Consider the Objection
Razi anticipates an objection: doesn't the same logic apply to power? Wouldn't that make power more perfect than Allah? Reflect on why this objection might arise.
The objection targets the principle of comparing attributes to their subject.
3
Examine the Reply
Razi replies that power is an attribute that cannot exist without its owner, whereas space and direction are independent of the being that occupies them. Therefore, the objection does not apply.
The key difference: power is dependent, space is independent.
4
Grasp the Conclusion
True perfection is above-ness in terms of power and authority, not spatial location. This is the appropriate interpretation of Quranic verses about Allah being above His creation.
This aligns with the broader Islamic understanding of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إن الفوقية الحاصلة بسبب الجهة، ليست صفة المدح، لأن تلك الفوقية حاصلة للجهة والحيز بعينها وذاتها، وحاصلة للمتمكن في ذلك الحيز، ولو كانت الفوقية بالجهة صفة مدح، لزم أن الحيز بسبب تلك الجهة أفضل وأكمل من الله تعالى. ولا يقال: يلزمكم أن تقولوا: بأن القدرة أفضل وأكمل من الله تعالى. لأنا نقول: القدرة صفة القادر، وممتنعة الوجود بدونه، بخلاف الحيز والجهة. فإنه غني عن الممكن، فثبت: أن الكمال والفضيلة إنما يحصلان بسبب الفوقية، بمعنى القدرة والسلطنة. فكان حمل الآية عليه أولى.
The third: Indeed, the above-ness that results from direction is not an attribute of praise, because that above-ness is obtained for the direction and space itself and its essence, and it is obtained for the occupant in that space. And if above-ness by direction were an attribute of praise, it would necessarily follow that the space, due to that direction, is better and more perfect than Allah, exalted. And it cannot be said: It is necessary for you to say that power is better and more perfect than Allah. For we say: Power is an attribute of the powerful, and its existence is impossible without Him, unlike space and direction. For He is independent of the contingent. So it is established that perfection and excellence are only obtained through above-ness in the sense of power and authority. So interpreting the verse according to that is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument, which is a logical and theological refutation of a literal spatial 'above-ness' for Allah. Razi argues that directional above-ness is not a praiseworthy attribute because it belongs equally to the location (space) itself and to whatever occupies that space. If such above-ness were a perfection, then the location (which is a created entity) would be superior to Allah—an absurd conclusion. A potential objection is raised: if we say power is a perfection, does that not make power superior to Allah? Razi answers that power is an attribute of the Powerful and cannot exist independently; it depends on its possessor. In contrast, space and direction are independent of the contingent occupant (they exist even without the occupant). Therefore, true perfection for Allah is above-ness in the sense of power and authority, not spatial location. Thus, interpreting Quranic verses about 'fawq' as power is the more appropriate understanding.

Key Terms
الجهة
الحيز
المدح
الكمال
الفضيلة
القدرة
السلطنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial above-ness is a praiseworthy attribute for Allah and indicates His greatness.
Correction: Razi proves that spatial above-ness would make the space (a creation) superior to Allah, so it cannot be a true perfection. True 'above-ness' for Allah is in the sense of power and authority.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that spatial above-ness cannot be a praiseworthy attribute for God?
A) Because God is not in space
B) Because the space itself would then be more excellent than God
C) Because no one can be above God
D) Because the Quran does not use spatial language

Key Takeaway
Spatial above-ness is not a true perfection; Allah's true 'above-ness' is His absolute power and authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's 'above-ness,' do you instinctively imagine a spatial direction? How can you train your mind to replace that with the reality of His absolute power and authority?
?
Razi warns that attributing spatial above-ness to Allah would make the space itself more perfect than Him. Have you ever, even unintentionally, ascribed a created quality to Allah? What step will you take to purify your understanding of His attributes?
?
If someone asked you, 'Where is Allah?' based on this lesson, how would you answer? Write a short, precise response that reflects true divine transcendence.

Razi's Grammatical Reinterpretation of the Angels Verse
Razi offers an alternative interpretation of the verse about angels fearing above.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi reinterprets a potentially anthropomorphic verse to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Grammar, not location: Razi reattaches 'from above them' to 'fear' not 'Lord.'

The verse describing angels fearing their Lord from above them is sometimes cited to suggest Allah is spatially above. Imam Razi offers an alternative grammatical reading that removes any implication of direction or place. Let's see how he does it.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Razi's alternative
First, read the Arabic text of the verse and Razi's proposed reinterpretation. Notice the phrase 'min fawqihim' (from above them).
The verse is from Surah an-Nahl, but Razi focuses on its syntactic structure.
2
Identify the grammatical shift
Razi suggests that 'min fawqihim' is connected to the verb 'yakhāfūna' (they fear) rather than to 'rabbahum' (their Lord). This changes the meaning from 'they fear their Lord who is above them' to 'they fear punishment coming from above them.'
This is a plausible reading in Arabic grammar, as prepositional phrases can attach to verbs or nouns.
3
Appreciate the theological motive
Why does Razi offer this interpretation? To uphold tanzih (divine transcendence) and avoid attributing spatial location or direction to Allah. The reinterpretation shows that the verse does not necessitate Allah being physically above.
This is a common method in kalam: using linguistic analysis to defend core beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله تعالى في صفة الملائكة: «يخافون ربهم من فوقهم»، ففيه جواب آخر: وهو أنه يحتمل أن يكون قوله: «من فوقهم»، صلة لقوله: «يخافون»، أي يخافون من فوقهم ربهم. وذلك لأنهم يخافون نزول العذاب عليهم من جانب فوقهم.
As for His statement, exalted, concerning the description of the angels: 'They fear their Lord from above them,' there is another answer: it is possible that His statement 'from above them' is connected to His statement 'they fear,' meaning they fear from above them their Lord. That is because they fear the descent of punishment upon them from above them.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific verse often used by opponents to claim that Allah is spatially above, since angels are said to fear their Lord from above them. Razi offers an alternative grammatical interpretation: the phrase 'from above them' (min fawqihim) can be attached to the verb 'they fear' (yakhāfūna) rather than to 'their Lord' (rabbahum). The meaning would then be that the angels fear punishment descending from above them, not that Allah Himself is located above them. This exegetical move preserves divine transcendence by reinterpreting the verse's syntactic structure. Razi does not claim this is the only possible interpretation, but presents it as a valid alternative that avoids anthropomorphism.

Razi's Refutation: 'Uluww' Means Power, Not Spatial Height
Razi argues that 'uluww' can mean power, not spatial height, using Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that verses with 'uluww' imply Allah's spatial location, by showing that 'uluww' often means power and dominance in Arabic usage.

Core Idea
Height in the Quran can mean power, not physical elevation.

Imam Razi now addresses the third objection of those who claim spatial direction for Allah: verses that mention 'uluww' (height). He does not deny that 'uluww' can have a spatial meaning, but he shows that it is used metaphorically for power and sovereignty. This lesson unpacks his linguistic and Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's answer
Read the passage to see the objection and Razi's immediate response that 'uluww' has two meanings.
2
Examine the linguistic evidence
Notice the examples Razi gives: sultan, highest court, etc. They all refer to authority, not space.
Razi uses common Arabic usage to show that 'uluww' can mean power.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Razi cites four Quranic verses where 'a'lā' is used non-spatially. Identify each and see how they support his argument.
Even Pharaoh's claim is about power, not physical height.
4
Reflect on the hermeneutic principle
Consider why this argument is effective against the opponents: they assume a literal spatial interpretation, but Razi shows the figurative usage is primary in many Quranic contexts.
This is a key move in kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي ذكروه ثالثا: وهو التمسك بالآيات المشتملة على لفظ العلو: فالجواب: إن لفظ العلو كما يستعمل في العلو بسبب الجهة، فقد يستعمل أيضاً في العلو بسبب القدرة. فإنه يقال: السلطان أعلى من غيره، ويكتب في أمثلة السلاطين: الديوان الأعلى، ويقال لأمرهم الأمر الأعلى، ويقال مجالسهم: المجلس الأعلى. والمراد في الكل: العلو، بمعنى: القهر والقدرة. لا بسبب المكان والجهة. وأيضاً: قال الله تعالى لموسى: «لا تخف إنك أنت الأعلى»، وقال: «ولا تهنوا ولا تحزنوا، وأنتم الأعلون»، وقال: «وكلمة الله هي العليا»، وقال فرعون: «أنا ربكم الأعلى»، والعلو في هذه المواضع بمعنى العلو بالقدرة، لا بمعنى العلو بالجهة.
As for what they mentioned thirdly—that is, clinging to the verses that contain the word 'uluww' (height): the answer is that the word 'uluww' is used for height due to direction, but it is also used for height due to power. For it is said: 'The sultan is higher than others,' and in the examples of sultans it is written: 'the highest court,' and for their command: 'the highest command,' and their councils: 'the highest council.' The intended [meaning] in all is height in the sense of dominance and power, not due to place and direction. Also, Allah, exalted, said to Musa: 'Fear not, indeed you are the highest.' And He said: 'And do not weaken nor grieve, for you are the uppermost.' And He said: 'And the word of Allah is the highest.' And Pharaoh said: 'I am your highest lord.' And height in these places is in the sense of height in power, not in the sense of height in direction.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage responds to the third objection of the opponents, who cite verses using the word 'uluww' (height/sublimity) as proof that Allah is spatially above. Razi counters by noting that 'uluww' has both a literal spatial meaning and a metaphorical meaning of power and dominance. He provides linguistic evidence from common usage: sultans are called 'higher', their courts 'the highest', their commands 'the highest command', etc. All these connote authority, not spatial location. He then cites Quranic verses where 'a'lā' (highest) is clearly non-spatial: Allah tells Musa he is 'the highest' (with power over Pharaoh), believers are 'the uppermost', Allah's word is 'the highest', and even Pharaoh's claim 'I am your highest lord' is a claim of supremacy, not a claim of being physically above. Thus, the verses that opponents use actually support interpretation as power, not spatial height.

The First Proof: Allah's Height is Intrinsic
Razi gives a rational proof that Allah's height is intrinsic, not spatial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's rational proof that Allah's quality of being 'Most High' is an intrinsic attribute of His essence, not a spatial location.

Core Idea
Allah's height is not spatial; it is a necessary attribute of His essence.

Imam Razi begins by quoting the Quranic verse 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High' (al-A'la 1). He argues that if Allah is higher than everything else, and direction is something other than Him, then Allah must be higher than direction itself. From this, he deduces that Allah's height cannot be due to direction—it must be intrinsic. He then anticipates an objection about the existence of direction and refutes it, grounding his proof in rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: verse, implication, conclusion, objection, rebuttal.
2
Identify the premises
Find the two key premises: (1) Allah is higher than everything else; (2) Direction is something other than Allah. How do these lead to the conclusion?
3
Understand the objection
The opponent questions whether direction is an 'existent thing' to be included in 'everything.' Reflect on why this objection might arise.
4
See how Razi refutes it
Razi appeals to rational proofs (dalā'il 'aqliyya) to assert that direction is indeed existent. Consider why this refutation is necessary.
This step shows that kalam uses reason alongside revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: إنه تعالى قال: «سبح اسم ربك الأعلى»، فحكم بأنه تعالى أعلى من كل ما سواه. والجهة شيء سواه، فوجب أن تكون ذاته أعلى من الجهة. وما كان أعلى من الجهة، يمتنع أن يكون علوه بسبب الجهة. فثبت: أن علوه لنفس ذاته، لا بسبب الجهة. ولا يقال: الجهة ليست بشيء موجود حتى تدخل تحت قوله: - سبح اسم ربك الأعلى. لأنا نقول: قد بينا في باب الدلائل العقلية: أنها لابد وأن تكون أمراً موجوداً.
The first: He, exalted, said: 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High.' So He ruled that He, exalted, is higher than everything other than Himself. And direction is something other than Him, so it is necessary that His essence be higher than direction. And whatever is higher than direction, it is impossible that its height be due to direction. So it is established that His height is for His own essence, not due to direction. And it cannot be said: direction is not an existent thing to be included under His saying: 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High.' For we say: we have already shown in the chapter of rational proofs that it must necessarily be an existent thing.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational proof that Allah's attribute of 'a'lā' (Most High) is not spatial. Razi quotes the Quranic verse 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High' (al-A'la 1). The verse declares that Allah is higher than everything else. Direction (al-jiha) is something other than Allah, so Allah must be higher than direction itself. If something is higher than direction, its height cannot be caused by direction—that would be circular. Therefore, Allah's height is intrinsic (li nafsihi), not derived from spatial location. A potential objection is raised: direction might not be considered an 'existent thing' (shay' mawjūd) to be included in 'everything' . Razi counters that in rational proofs (al-dalā'il al-'aqliyya), direction is indeed a real existent. Thus, the proof stands.

الجهة

الدلائل العقلية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'Allah is the Most High' means He is spatially above the universe or that direction does not exist at all.
Correction: Razi's argument shows that direction exists as a created thing, but Allah's height is not due to direction; it is an intrinsic attribute of His essence, transcending spatial categorization.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Allah's height is intrinsic to His essence, not derived from spatial direction, as proven by the Quran and rational consistency.

Razi argues that direction is something other than Allah and thus inferior. Have you ever mentally placed Allah in a direction, even if unconsciously? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your belief? Will you actively reject any thought that confines Allah to a direction?
?
The proof shows that Allah's height is due to His own essence, not derived from anything else. Do you ever base your feeling of Allah's greatness on external things like magnificent nature or huge numbers? How can you instead anchor your awe in the intrinsic majesty of His essence, independent of His creations? What practical step will you take this week to cultivate that awareness?

The Meaning of 'Uluww: Power, Not Spatial Height
Razi explains that 'uluww can mean power, not spatial height.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic word 'uluww (highness) can mean power and domination, not necessarily physical elevation, and how this applies to Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
'Uluww means power, not location — Allah is high in might, not in space.

Imam al-Razi addresses a key objection: do verses describing Allah as 'high' imply He is in a direction? He demonstrates that the Arabic word 'uluww has two meanings: spatial height and power/domination. By examining linguistic usage and Quranic examples, he shows that when applied to Allah, 'uluww refers to His absolute power, not physical location. This lesson walks through his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Recognize that opponents cite verses with 'uluww to argue that Allah is in a spatial direction. This is the third argument Razi responds to.
The objection is mentioned at the beginning of the passage.
2
Grasp the two meanings of 'uluww
Razi states that 'uluww can be due to direction or due to power. This distinction is crucial for the response.
3
Examine Arabic usage examples
Read the examples: sultan, highest chancery, highest command, highest assembly. These show 'uluww used for authority, not location.
These are conventional phrases in Arabic.
4
See Quranic verses as evidence
Razi quotes four verses where 'uluww clearly means power: Moses, believers, the word of Allah, and Pharaoh's claim.
Note that Pharaoh used it falsely, but still in the sense of power.
5
Conclude the meaning for Allah
Allah's 'uluww is by power and domination, not by spatial direction. This preserves divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي ذكروه ثالثا . وهو التمسك بالآيات المشتملة على لفظ العلو : فالجواب : إن لفظ العلو كما يستعمل فى العلو يسبب الجهة ، فقد يستعمل أيضاً في العلو بسبب القدرة . فإنه يقال : السلطان أعلى من غيره ، ويكتب في أمثلة السلاطين : الديوان الأعلى . ويقال لأمرهم (٦٧) الأمر الأعلى (ويقال (٦٨)) مجالسهم : المجلس الأعلى . والمراد في الكل : العلو ، بمعنى : القهر والقدرة . لا بسبب المكان والجهة .
وأيضاً : قال الله تعالى لموسى : « لا تخف إنك أنت الأعلى (٦٩) وقال : [د؟] ولا تهنوا ولا تحزنوا ، وأنتم الأعلون (۷۰) ، وقال : « وكلمة الله هي العليا (٧١) ، وقال فرعون : ( أنا ربكم الأعلى (۷۲) ، والعلو في هذه المواضع بمعنى العلو بالقدرة ، لا بمعنى العلو بالجهة .
As for what they mentioned thirdly – namely, clinging to verses containing the word 'uluww (highness) – the answer is: The word 'uluww, just as it is used for highness due to direction, is also used for highness due to power. For it is said: 'The sultan is higher than others,' and in examples of sultans it is written: 'the highest chancery.' And their command is called 'the highest command,' and their assemblies: 'the highest assembly.' The intent in all is highness meaning domination (al-qahr) and power (al-qudra), not due to place and direction. Also, Allah Almighty said to Moses: 'Fear not, indeed you are the highest' (20:68), and said: 'Do not weaken nor grieve, for you are the highest' (3:139), and said: 'And the word of Allah is the highest' (9:40), and Pharaoh said: 'I am your highest lord' (79:24). Highness in these places is highness by power, not highness by direction.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of Razi's response to the third argument of those who claim God is in a direction by citing verses with the word 'uluww (highness). The author first distinguishes two meanings of 'uluww: one due to spatial direction (al-jihat) and one due to power (al-qudra) and domination (al-qahr). To demonstrate the linguistic validity of the non-spatial meaning, he gives examples from common usage: a sultan is called 'higher' by virtue of authority, not location, and terms like 'the highest chancery' (al-dīwān al-a'lā) and 'the highest assembly' (al-majlis al-a'lā) are conventional. Then he cites Quranic verses where 'uluww is clearly used in the sense of power: Allah's words to Moses 'you are the highest,' to the believers 'you are the highest,' saying 'the word of Allah is the highest,' and Pharaoh's boast 'I am your highest lord.' In all these, the meaning is superiority in power, not spatial elevation. This establishes that the presence of 'uluww in a verse does not necessarily imply direction for Allah.

The Verse 'Most High' Proves Transcendence Beyond Direction
Razi's first proof from the Qur'an against spatial direction for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi derives from the verse 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High' that Allah's highness is inherent to His essence, not caused by any direction.

Core Idea
Allah is higher than direction itself, so His highness cannot come from direction.

This lesson unpacks Razi's first rational proof from the Qur'an. He takes the verse 'سبح اسم ربك الأعلى' (Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High) and shows that it logically entails that Allah's highness is not spatial or directional. The proof involves a simple but powerful argument: if Allah is higher than everything other than Him, and direction is something other than Him, then He is higher than direction. Therefore, direction cannot be the source of His highness. Razi also preempts an objection about the existence of direction itself, referring to the rational proofs that establish direction as an existing thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the original argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the verse quoted and the main conclusion Razi draws from it.
Notice how Razi presents multiple proofs—this is the first (الأول).
2
Trace the logical steps
Follow Razi's reasoning: 1) Allah is higher than everything else. 2) Direction is something else. 3) So Allah is higher than direction. 4) Whatever is higher than direction cannot have its highness from direction. 5) Therefore, His highness is self-inherent.
Write out the syllogism in your own words.
3
Consider the objection and response
Note the objection: 'Direction is not an existing thing, so the verse doesn't apply to it.' Razi counters by asserting that rational proofs show direction exists. Ask yourself: why must direction exist for the argument to work?
This shows the integration of rational theology with scriptural interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الذى يدل على أن المراد ما ذكرناه : وجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى قال : « سبح اسم ربك الأعلى (۷۳) ، فحكم بأنه تعالى أعلى من كل ما سواه . والجهة شيء سواه ، فوجب أن تكون ذاته أعلى من الجهة . وما كان أعلى من الجهة ، يمتنع أن يكون علوه بسبب الجهة . فثبت : أن علوه لنفس ذاته ، لا بسبب الجهة . ولا يقال : الجهة ليست بشيء موجود حتى تدخل تحت قوله : - سبح اسم ربك الأعلى . لأنا نقول : قد بينا في باب الدلائل العقلية : أنها لابد وأن تكون أمراً موجوداً .
What indicates that the intended meaning is what we have mentioned are aspects: First: He Almighty said: 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High' (87:1). So He ruled that He Almighty is higher than everything other than Him. Direction is something other than Him, so it is necessary that His essence be higher than direction. And whatever is higher than direction, it is impossible that its highness be due to direction. So it is established that His highness is for His own essence, not due to direction. And it cannot be said: Direction is not an existing thing to be included under His saying 'the Most High.' For we say: We have already shown in the chapter on rational proofs that it must be an existing thing.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs (wujuh) that the intended meaning of 'uluww for Allah is not spatial direction. The proof is based on the verse 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High' (al-A'la 87:1). Razi argues that Allah is declared 'higher than everything other than Him' (a'la min kull ma siwah). Since direction (al-jihat) is something other than Him, His essence must be higher than direction. Therefore, if His highness were due to direction, He would be dependent on something other than Himself and would not be higher than it. Hence, His highness must be inherent to His essence (li-nafs dhatihi), not acquired from direction. A potential objection: 'Direction is not an existing thing, so the verse does not apply to it.' Razi counters by stating that in the chapter on rational proofs (bab al-dala'il al-'aqliyya), it has already been demonstrated that direction (i.e., empty space) must be an existing thing (amr mawjud). Thus, direction is included in 'everything other than Him,' and the argument holds.

الجهة
الدلائل العقلية
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The verse 'سبح اسم ربك الأعلى' means Allah is spatially above the heavens.
Correction: Razi shows that the verse actually proves Allah is above direction itself, implying His highness is not spatial but essential.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic description 'Most High' indicates that Allah's highness is essential, not spatial or directional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The verse commands you to glorify 'the Most High.' When you utter these words, do you truly conceive of Allah as beyond all direction, or do you still imagine a 'somewhere' for Him? What would it cost you to let go of every spatial image and worship a God who is high in essence alone?

Razi argues that if Allah's highness came from direction, He would be dependent on it. Is there any created thing – a person, a place, a status – that you are depending on for your sense of spiritual elevation? What would it mean to depend solely on Allah's essential greatness?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is the Most High. Yet what direction does your heart lean toward when you seek comfort or success? Are you still turning to created directions instead of the One who is above all direction? How will you reorient your life starting today?

Divine Transcendence

The Dilemma of Direction: Finitude or Infinitude
Razi's dilemma against direction for Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing a direction to Allah leads to absurdities, whether we assume He has a limit or not.

Core Idea
Direction for God leads to an inescapable dilemma with impossible consequences.

Imam Razi presents a crisp logical argument against the idea that Allah could be in a direction. He sets up a dilemma: if Allah were in a direction, He would either have a limit (finitude) or not (infinitude) in that direction. Both options lead to contradictions—either something higher than Allah, or division and change in His essence. This lesson unpacks that dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma setup
Read the passage carefully to grasp the two horns: either Allah has a limit in the direction above, or He does not. Notice how Razi presents these as exhaustive possibilities.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Analyze the first horn: finitude
Examine why if Allah had a limit, there would be empty spaces above Him, making something else higher. Also, He could create a world above, contradicting His supremacy.
Empty spaces (al-ahiyaz al-khaliyya) are considered possible locations.
3
Analyze the second horn: infinitude
Consider the implication that Allah's essence would then have both a finite side and an infinite side, leading to composition (tarkib) and change—impossible for God.
This horn argues that the very notion of 'direction' entails parts in God's essence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note that both horns lead to falsehood, so the original assumption (that Allah is in a direction) must be false. This reaffirms tanzih (transcendence).
The argument does not depend on any specific direction; it works for any direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : هو أنه تعالى لو كان في جهة فوق . فإما أن يكون له في جهة فوق : نهاية ، وإما أن لا يكون له فى تلك الجهة : نهاية ، فإن كان الأول لم يكن أعلى الأشياء لان الأحياز الخالية فوقه تكون أعلى منه ، ولأنه قادر على خلق الأجسام في جميع الأحياز. فيكون قادراً على خلق عالم في تلك الأحياز التي هي فوقه ، فيكون ذلك العالم على ذلك التقدير أعلى منه. وإنما قلنا : لانهاية لذات الله تعالى من جهة فوق ، لأن هذا الجانب المتناهى منه ، مخالف فى الماهية للجانب الذي هو غير متناه ، ولا يصح على كل واحد منهما ماصح على الآخر ، وصح أن ينقلب غير المتناهي متناهيا، والمتناهى غير متناه . وذلك يقتضى جواز الفصل والوصل في ذات الله تعالى . وهو محال .
Second: If He Almighty were in a direction above, then either He has a limit (nihaya) in the direction above, or He has no limit in that direction. If the first, then He would not be the highest of things, because the empty spaces above Him would be higher than Him, and because He is able to create bodies in all spaces. So He would be able to create a world in those spaces that are above Him, and that world would on that assumption be higher than Him. And we say: the essence of Allah has no limit from the direction above, because this finite side of Him would be different in essence (mukhalif fi al-mahiyya) from the side that is infinite, and what is valid for each one is not valid for the other, and it would be possible for the infinite to become finite and the finite infinite. That would entail the permissibility of separation and connection (al-fasl wa al-wasl) in the essence of Allah, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is the second proof against spatial direction for Allah. Razi presents a dilemma: if Allah were in a direction (e.g., above), either He has a limit (nihaya) in that direction or He does not. If He has a limit, then the empty spaces (al-ahiyaz al-khaliyya) above Him would be higher, or He could create a world in those spaces, making that world higher than Him – both contradict His being the highest (a'la al-ashya'). If He has no limit, then His essence would have a finite side (the side we consider) and an infinite side. These two sides would differ in essence, and properties valid for one would not hold for the other. Moreover, it would be possible for the infinite to become finite and the finite infinite, implying change. This would entail that separation and connection (al-fasl wa al-wasl) are possible in Allah's essence, meaning composition (tarkib) and divisibility, which are impossible for God (muhal). Thus, both horns of the dilemma lead to absurdity, proving that Allah is not in any direction.

Key Terms
النهاية
الأحياز
الماهية
المتناهي
غير المتناهي
الفصل والوصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being infinite in a direction is a positive attribute showing God's greatness.
Correction: Razi shows that infinitude in a direction actually entails a finite side in the essence, leading to composition and change, which are impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue would follow if Allah had no limit in the direction above?
A) He would be the highest of all things
B) His essence would be divisible into finite and infinite parts, leading to compositeness
C) He would be able to create worlds anywhere
D) He would be limited by empty spaces

Key Takeaway
The only way to avoid absurdities is to affirm that Allah is not in any direction—He transcends spatial categories entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever unconsciously imagine Allah as being 'above' in a literal sense? How does Razi's dilemma challenge that mental picture, and what will you do to train yourself to think of Allah as transcendent beyond all directions?
?
Razi says that if Allah had a limit, something could be higher than Him. Do you ever act as if something in your life is 'higher' than Allah—like status, wealth, or your own desires? How will you use this lesson to re-establish His absolute supremacy in your heart?
?
If believing in a direction for God leads to absurdities like composition and change, what does that mean for your worship? Will you commit to studying more about Allah's attributes so that your worship is based on truth, not on comfortable images?

Third proof: If God were infinite upward, no part could be the highest
Razi argues that an infinite being cannot be the highest in that direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that Allah's highness cannot be due to direction, because an infinite being in a direction cannot be the highest.

Core Idea
An infinite being in a direction can never be the highest.

In this third and final proof, Razi closes the argument against directional highness. He shows that even if we imagine Allah as infinite in the upward direction, that very infinitude prevents Him from being described as the highest—because there would always be another part above. This forces us to conclude that Allah's highness transcends space and direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, reasoning, conclusion.
The passage is concise; read carefully to grasp the argument.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument goes: if infinite upward, then for every part there is a part above it. So no part is the highest. Therefore, a directional being cannot be the highest. Since Allah is the highest, His highness is not directional.
This is a conditional proof: if P then Q, and Q leads to conclusion.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that Allah's highness is not by direction or space (jihat or hayyiz). Connect this to the broader theme of divine transcendence.
This proof complements the previous two by showing the impossibility of being highest in a direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنه إذا كان غير متناه من جانب الفوق ، فلاجزء إلا وفوقه جزء آخر . وكل ما فوقه غيره : لم يكن أعلى الموجودات . فإذن ليس في تلك الأجزاء شيء هو أعلى الموجودات . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كل ما كان مختصاً بالجهة ، فإنه لا يمكن وصفه بأنه أعلى الموجودات . وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون علوه - تعالى لا بالجهة والحيز . وهو المطلوب .
Third: If He is infinite from the direction above, then there is no part except that above it is another part. And everything above it is other than it: it would not be the highest of existents. Thus, there is nothing in those parts that is the highest of existents. So it is established by what we have mentioned that everything that is particularized by direction cannot be described as the highest of existents. And if that is so, then it is necessary that His highness – exalted is He – be not by direction and space. And that is what is intended.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is the third and final proof that Allah's highness is not due to direction. Razi continues from the previous proof: if Allah were infinite in the upward direction (ghayr mutanahi min janib al-fawq), then for every part (juz') there is another part above it. Consequently, no part can be the highest (a'la al-mawjudat), because every part has something above it. Therefore, any being particularized by direction (mukhtass bi al-jihat) cannot be described as the highest of existents. Since Allah is undoubtedly the highest, His highness must be independent of direction and space (al-jihat wa al-hayyiz). This concluding proof reinforces the previous dilemma by showing that even if we accept infinitude in a direction, the attribute of being 'the highest' would be impossible for such a being. Thus, the only consistent position is that Allah transcends spatial attributes altogether.

Key Terms
غير متناه
الجزء
أعلى الموجودات
الجهة
الحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'infinite in a direction' is a way to be highest.
Correction: Razi shows that if something is infinite in a direction, there will always be a part above it, so it cannot be the highest of existents.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the third proof?
A) Allah is infinite in all directions
B) Allah cannot be described as the highest if He is in a direction
C) Allah's highness is due to His infinite parts
D) Allah's highness is spatial but unlimited

Key Takeaway
Allah's highness is not by direction because a direction-bound being cannot be the highest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that if Allah were bound by direction, He could not be the Highest. Do you ever imagine Allah as being 'up there' in a physical sense, despite knowing He is beyond space? What practical step can you take to purify your concept of Allah's transcendence?
?
If every direction-bound thing has something above it, then nothing in creation can be truly supreme. How does this affect your attachment to worldly status or positions? Will you consciously lower yourself today in humility before the One who is above all directions?
?
Razi's argument requires rigorous thinking to defend tawhid. Are you willing to study such proofs even when they challenge comfortable mental images of Allah? Commit to one concrete action to deepen your understanding of Allah's attributes.

Razi's Response to the Ascent Verses
Razi responds to the fourth argument by analysing the word 'uruuj and noting the verses' lack of specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that verses mentioning 'uruuj (ascent) do not prove that Allah has a spatial direction.

Core Idea
Ascent verses do not specify what the ascent is for, so they don't imply a location for Allah.

The opponents of divine transcendence used Quranic verses mentioning 'ascent' to argue that Allah is physically above. Razi responds by examining the language itself, showing that the verses do not tell us what the ascent is for. This lesson walks through his linguistic and logical response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi first defines the word ma'arij and then points out the lack of specification.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is complete.
2
Identify the two parts of Razi's response
First, Razi explains that ma'arij means ladders or means of ascent. Second, he argues that the verses do not specify what the ascent is for, so they cannot prove direction.
3
Reflect on the burden of proof
Razi's argument shifts the burden to the objector: they must show the ascent implies a location for Allah. Since the verses are silent, their proof collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدى تمسكوا به رابعاً: وهو الآيات المشتملة على لفظ العروج . كقوله تعالى : « يدبر الأمر من السماء إلى الأرض . ثم يعرج إليه (٧٤) ، وقوله : « ذى المعارج تعرج الملائكة والروح (٧٥) إليه ، فجوابه : إن المعارج جمع معرج، وهو المصعد، ومنه قوله تعالى : « ومعارج عليم ) يظهر ون (٧٦) ، وليس فى هذه الآيات (۷۷) بيان أن تلك المعارج: معارج لأى شيء ؟ فسقطت حجتهم في هذا الباب . بل يجوز أن تكون تلك
As for what they clung to fourthly: the verses containing the word 'uruuj (ascent), like His saying: 'He arranges the affair from the heaven to the earth, then it ascends to Him' (32:5), and His saying: 'Lord of the ascents (al-ma'arij) by which the angels and the Spirit ascend to Him' (70:3-4), the answer is: Al-ma'arij is the plural of mi'raj, which is the ascent-ladder, and from it is His saying: 'And lofty ascent-ladders (ma'arij)' (43:33). And in these verses there is no indication that those ascents are ascents for what? So their proof in this matter falls away. Rather, it is permissible that those... (text cut off)
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of Razi's response to a fourth argument based on verses containing the word 'uruuj (ascent) or ma'arij (ascents). The objectors cited verses like 'then it ascends to Him' (32:5) and 'the angels and the Spirit ascend to Him' (70:3-4) to argue that God is in a direction (upward). Razi's answer has two parts. First, he explains the lexical meaning: ma'arij is the plural of mi'raj, meaning a ladder or means of ascent (al-mis'ad). He cites another verse (43:33) where the word is used for literal ladders. Second, he points out that these verses do not specify what the ascent is for (li-ayy shay'); i.e., they do not indicate that the destination of the ascent is a physical location. Therefore, the verses do not prove that God is in a direction. The passage ends abruptly (due to page break), but the argument is that the burden of proof is on the objectors to show that the ascent implies a spatial direction for God, which they have not done. Thus, their proof collapses ('saqatat hujjatuhum').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that verses about ascent (e.g., 'then it ascends to Him') prove that Allah is physically above.
Correction: Razi shows that these verses do not specify what the ascent is for; they only mention the act of ascending, not its destination or direction.

Key Takeaway

إذا كنت قد تصورت الله في السماء كأنه جالس على عرش مكاني، فما هو الأثر الذي تركته فكرة الرازي على تعظيمك لله؟ هل تستطيع الآن أن تعبده دون أن تحدده بمكان؟ وماذا يقتضي ذلك من تغيير في خشوعك؟

Interpreting the Ascent of Angels: The Meaning of 'Ila'
Author explains the meaning of 'ila' in the angels' ascent verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the phrase 'ascend to Him' in the Quran does not imply spatial direction or location for Allah, but rather signifies the ultimate return of all affairs to His will and command.

Core Idea
Ascension to Allah is about submission to His will, not physical movement toward a place.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses a common misinterpretation of the verse describing angels ascending to Allah. He shows that the Arabic preposition 'ila' (to) here does not indicate a spatial endpoint, but rather the termination of matters to Allah's will. This is a crucial point for preserving divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the structure: first definitions of ma'arij, then the main point about 'ila', then analogies.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where the author says what 'ila' does NOT mean and what it DOES mean. Underline the sentence.
3
Examine the analogies
Look at the two examples the author gives: the Quranic verse 'and to Him returns the whole affair' and Abraham's statement. How do they support the claim?
Both show that 'to' can indicate destination of command, not space.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Why is it important that Allah is not associated with direction or place? Connect to the principle of tanzih.
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعارج : معارج النعم الله تعالى ، أو معارج للملائكة، أو معارج لأهل الثواب واما قوله تعالى: «تعرج الملائكة والروح إليه»، فنقول : ليس المراد من حرف "إلى" في قوله "إليه" المكان . بل المراد : انتهاء الأمور إلى مراده . ونظيره : قوله تعالى : ( وإليه يرجع الأمر كله )، والمراد : انتهاء أهل الثواب إلى منازل العز والكرامة. كقول إبراهيم: إني ذاهب إلى ربي سيهدين، ويكون هذا إشارة إلى أن دار الثواب أعلى الأمكنة وأرفعها ، بالنسبة إلى أكثر المخلوقات.
Al-Ma'arij: the ascents of blessings from Allah the Exalted, or ascents for the angels, or ascents for the people of reward. And as for His statement the Exalted: 'The angels and the Spirit ascend to Him,' we say: the intended meaning of the particle 'to' in His statement 'to Him' is not place. Rather, the intended meaning is the ending of matters to His will. And its analogy is His statement the Exalted: 'And to Him returns the whole affair,' and the intended meaning is the arrival of the people of reward to the stations of honor and dignity, like the saying of Abraham: 'I am going to my Lord; He will guide me.' And this is an indication that the abode of reward is the highest and most elevated of places relative to most creatures.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that uses the verse 'The angels and the Spirit ascend to Him' to claim that Allah is in a particular direction or place. The author begins by explaining the word 'ma'arij' (ascents) in three possible interpretations: as the ascents of blessings from Allah, or as ascents for angels, or as ascents for the people of reward. Then he addresses the verse about angels ascending. He argues that the word 'ila' (to) in 'ascend to Him' does not indicate physical location. Rather, it signifies the termination of affairs to His will and command. He supports this by citing another verse: 'And to Him returns the whole affair' (Hud 123), where the return is not spatial but refers to judgment and decree. Similarly, the verse about Abraham saying 'I am going to my Lord' does not mean a physical journey to a place where Allah resides, but rather a journey towards fulfilling His command. Finally, he concludes that this interpretation indicates that paradise (the abode of reward) is the highest and most honored place among creation, not that Allah is located there.

Key Terms
المعارج
العروج
حرف إلى
المكان
الانتهاء إلى المراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'ascension to Allah' implies spatial proximity or location for Allah.
Correction: The preposition 'ila' indicates the termination of affairs to Allah's will, not spatial direction.

Checkpoint
What is the intended meaning of 'ila' in the verse about angels ascending?
A) Physical direction towards Allah's location
B) The end of matters to His command and will
C) Movement in space
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'ascend to Him' in the Quran should be understood as a reference to the ultimate return of all matters to Allah's will, not as a spatial movement towards a location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You claim that 'to Him returns the whole affair'—but honestly, do you live each day as if your plans, failures, and successes ultimately end in His will, or do you still grasp for control as if the outcome depended on you?
?
Abraham said 'I am going to my Lord'—in what concrete way are you 'going to your Lord' today through your choices and actions? Is your journey one of obedient submission, or are you walking in a direction of your own making?
