Shared Names Do Not Imply Anthropomorphism
Imam Razi clarifies that shared names do not necessitate tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why applying the same terms to Allah and creation does not imply anthropomorphism, based on the distinction between attributes and essence.

Core Idea
Shared names reflect attributes, not essence.

Imam Razi addresses a common accusation: that using terms like 'existent' for Allah and creation implies comparing Allah to creation. He first presents the opponents' defense, then delivers his own argument that the real issue is about essence, not attributes. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow. Notice that it contains two voices: the opponents' claim and Razi's rebuttal.
Pay attention to the shift after 'واعلم : أن حاصل هذا الكلام من جانبنا'.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
Identify what the opponents (the group being accused of tashbih) say in their defense. They argue that using common terms does not make one a mushabbih, and they deny essential similarity.
They concede similarity only in some attributes, not in essence.
3
Understand Razi's counterargument
Razi refutes by stating that if Allah were a body, He would be identical to other bodies in essence, because he has proven all bodies are identical in complete essence. This would entail tashbih.
He distinguishes between attributes (existence, knowledge, power) and essence (mahiyya).
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Notice that the debate hinges on whether similarity in attributes implies similarity in essence. Razi says no, but similarity in corporeality would imply essential similarity.
This distinction is central to Islamic theology.
5
Summarize the lesson
Write a one-sentence summary: Shared terms do not imply shared essence; only attributes are common, not the essential nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنه لانزاع فى أن لفظ : الموجود، والشيء الواحد، والذات، والمعلوم، والمذكور ، والعالم ، والقادر ، والحى ، والمريد ، والسميع ، والبصير ، والمتكلم ، والباقى : واقع على الحق سبحانه وتعالى، وعلى خلقه. ثبَت بما ذكرنا : أن المشابهة من بعض الوجوه لا توجب أن يكون قائله موصوفا : بأنه مشبه الله تعالى بالخلق ، وبأنه مشبه . ونحن لا نثبت المشابهة بينه وبين خلقه ، إلا فى بعض الصور والصفات إلا أنا نعتقد أنه تعالى ، وإن كان جسما ، إلا أنه بخلاف سائر الأجسام في ذاته وحقيقته فثبت : أن إطلاق اسم المشبه على هذه الطائفة : كذب وزور . هذا جملة كلامهم في هذا الباب. واعلم : أن حاصل هذا الكلام من جانبنا: أنا قد دللنا في القسم الأول من هذا الكتاب على أن الأجسام متماثلة فى تمام الماهية ( فلو كان الباري تعالى جسما لزم أن يكون مثلا لهذه الأجسام في تمام الماهية ( وحينئذ يكون القول بالتشبيه لازما . أما ما لم يدل الدليل على الشيء في الموجوديه والعالمية والقادرية ، فإنه لا يوجب تماثلها في تمام الماهية . فظهر الفرق (وبالله التوفيق)
And know: There is no dispute that the terms: 'the existent', 'the one thing', 'the essence', 'the known', 'the mentioned', 'the knowing', 'the powerful', 'the living', 'the willing', 'the hearing', 'the seeing', 'the speaking', 'the everlasting' apply to the Truth, exalted and high, and to His creation. It is established by what we mentioned: that similarity in some aspects does not necessitate that the speaker be described as one who compares Allah to creation, or as an anthropomorphist. And we do not affirm similarity between Him and His creation except in some forms and attributes, except that we believe that He, exalted, even if He were a body, is unlike other bodies in His essence and reality. So it is established that applying the name 'anthropomorphist' to this group is falsehood and slander. This is the summary of their speech in this chapter. And know: The gist of this speech from our side is: We have proven in the first part of this book that bodies are similar in the entirety of essence. So if the Creator, exalted, were a body, it would be necessary for Him to be similar to these bodies in the entirety of essence, and then the statement of anthropomorphism would be necessary. As for that which the proof does not indicate concerning a thing in existence, knowingness, and powerfulness, it does not necessitate their similarity in the entirety of essence. So the difference is clear (and with Allah is success).
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and rebuttal regarding the accusation of anthropomorphism (tashbih). The author first establishes consensus that certain terms (e.g., existent, knower, powerful) apply both to Allah and to created beings, but this does not imply that the user of such terms is guilty of tashbih. He notes that his opponents admit similarity only in some attributes, not in essence, and they consider calling them 'mushabbih' a lie. Then Imam Razi delivers his own argument: He has already proven that all bodies are identical in their complete essence (tamam al-mahiyya). Therefore, if Allah were a body, He would necessarily be identical in essence to other bodies, which would entail tashbih. However, attributes like existence, knowledge, and power do not require such essential similarity. Thus, the difference between affirming attributes and affirming a body is clear. The passage ends with 'and with Allah is success'.

Correction: The passage shows that shared names only indicate similarity in attributes, not in essence. Essential identity would require bodies to be identical in essence, which is not the case for attributes.

Two Opinions on Takfir of Body-Affirmers
Imam Razi presents two opinions on takfir of those who affirm jism and jiha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly opinions on whether those who affirm a body and direction for Allah are disbelievers, along with the reasoning behind the first opinion and its contrast with the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Affirming a body for Allah denies His existence, not just His attributes.

Imam Razi presents a critical debate on whether those who attribute a body and direction to Allah are to be considered disbelievers. He outlines two opinions, with the first emphasizing that such a belief effectively denies Allah's true essence. This lesson examines the arguments and contrasts with the Mu'tazila, whose disagreement is only about attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall structure. Notice that it begins by stating the question and then lists two opinions.
The passage is incomplete; the second opinion is cut off.
2
Identify the two opinions
Locate the first opinion ("أحدهما: أنه كافر") and the second opinion ("والقول الثاني: إنا لا نكفرهم"). Understand the basic stance of each.
3
Analyze the reasoning of the first opinion
Follow the argument: anything with a direction is created, so affirming that for Allah implies He is created, which denies His true existence. The author contrasts this with the Mu'tazila, who affirm Allah's existence but differ on attributes.
The key point is that the Mujassima deny the very essence of Allah.
4
Consider the second opinion's incomplete reasoning
The second opinion suggests that if knowledge of transcendence were a condition for faith, the Prophet would not have judged some as believers. Reflect on what this implies about the scope of takfir.
The passage ends here, so we lack the full conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث فى أن من يثبت كونه تعالى جسما متحيزا مختصا بجهة معينة • هل يحكم بكفره أم لا ؟ للعلماء فيه قولان : أحدهما : أنه كافر - وهو الأظهر - وهذا لأن مذهبنا : أن كل شيء يكون مختصا بجهة وحيز ، فإنه مخلوق محدث ، وله إله أحدثه وخلقه . وأما القائلون بالجسمية والجهة الذين أنكروا وجود موجود آخر سوى هذه الأشياء التي يمكن الإشارة إليها ، فهم منكرون لذات الموجود ، الذى يعتقد أنه هو الإله . وإذا كانوا منكرين لذاته ، كانوا كفارا لا محالة . وهذا بخلاف المعتزلة فإنه يثبت موجودا ، وراء هذه الأشياء التى يشار إليها بالحس، إلا أنه خالفنا في صفات ذلك الموجود . والمجسمة يخالفوننا في إثبات ذات المعبود و وجوده ، فكان هذا الخلاف أعظم . فيلزمهم الكفر ، لكن فهم منكرين لذات المعبود الحق ولوجوده والمعتزلة في صفته ، لا في ذاته . والقول الثاني : إنا لا نكفرهم . لأن معرفة التنزيه ، لو كانت شرطا الصحة الإيمان لوجب على الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ، أن لا يحكم بإيمان
Chapter Three on whether he who affirms that the Exalted is a body, occupying space, specific to a certain direction – is he judged to be a disbeliever or not? Scholars have two opinions: One of them: that he is a disbeliever – and it is the more apparent – and this is because our doctrine is: every thing that is specific to a direction and a space, then it is created, originated, and has a god who originated and created it. As for those who affirm corporeality and direction who denied the existence of any other existent besides these things that can be pointed to, they are deniers of the essence of the Existent that is believed to be the god. And if they are deniers of His essence, they are disbelievers without exception. And this is unlike the Mu'tazila, for they affirm an existent beyond these things that are perceived by the senses, except that they differed from us concerning the attributes of that existent. And the Mujassima differ from us concerning affirming the essence of the Worshipped and His existence, so this difference is greater. So disbelief befalls them, but they are deniers of the essence of the true Worshipped and His existence, while the Mu'tazila [differ] concerning His attributes, not His essence. The second opinion: that we do not deem them disbelievers, because knowledge of transcendence, if it were a condition for the validity of faith, would require the Messenger, peace be upon him, not to judge as having faith...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of two scholarly opinions regarding the ruling on those who affirm that Allah is a physical body occupying a direction. The first opinion, considered more apparent, declares such a person a disbeliever. The reasoning is that anything occupying a direction is created, and those who affirm that for Allah effectively deny the existence of the true God, because they limit Him to physical existence. The author contrasts this with the Mu'tazila, who affirm Allah's existence but dispute His attributes. The Mujassima (those who affirm a body) are more severe because they contradict the very essence of God. The second opinion holds that they are not to be declared disbelievers, arguing that if knowledge of God's transcendence were a condition for valid faith, the Prophet would not have considered some believers as Muslims (the text cuts off, implying that some early Muslims may have held imperfect concepts of God). The passage is incomplete but presents both sides.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference between Mujassima (body-affirmers) and Mu'tazila is minor; both only disagree on attributes.
Correction: The Mujassima deny Allah's very existence by limiting Him to a physical body, while the Mu'tazila affirm His existence but differ on His attributes. The author emphasizes that this is a more fundamental disagreement.

Key Takeaway
Affirming a body for Allah is not merely a disagreement about attributes but a denial of Allah's unique existence.

The First Opinion: Anthropomorphists are Disbelievers
Razi presents the first opinion and its proof, contrasting anthropomorphists with Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument for why anthropomorphists are considered disbelievers, and how this differs from the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
To deny God's essence is disbelief; to deny His attributes is lesser error.

This passage from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis examines the critical issue of declaring anthropomorphists disbelievers. Razi presents the first and more apparent opinion that those who affirm God is a body occupying space deny His very essence, thus committing disbelief. He contrasts them with the Mu'tazila, who affirm God's existence but err in attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the logical flow from the premise to the conclusion.
2
Identify the core premise
Razi's argument rests on the premise that anything associated with a direction or space is created (makhluq). Understand this principle and why it is central.
This premise is a key doctrine in Islamic theology regarding God's transcendence.
3
Understand the contrast with Mu'tazila
Razi distinguishes anthropomorphists from the Mu'tazila: the former deny God's essence, the latter only deny some attributes. Grasp this distinction to see why the ruling differs.
This contrast highlights the severity of tajsim compared to other theological errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في أن من يثبت كونه تعالى جسما متحيزا مختصا بجهة معينة هل يحكم بكفره أم لا للعلماء فيه قولان أحدهما أنه كافر وهو الأظهر وهذا لأن مذهبنا أن كل شيء يكون مختصا بجهة وحيز فإنه مخلوق محدث وله إله أحدثه وخلقه وأما القائلون بالجسمية والجهة الذين أنكروا وجود موجود آخر سوى هذه الأشياء التي يمكن الإشارة إليها فهم منكرون لذات الموجود الذي يعتقد أنه هو الإله وإذا كانوا منكرين لذاته كانوا كفارا لا محالة وهذا بخلاف المعتزلة فإنه يثبت موجودا وراء هذه الأشياء التي يشار إليها بالحس إلا أنه خالفنا في صفات ذلك الموجود والمجسمة يخالفوننا في إثبات ذات المعبود ووجوده فكان هذا الخلاف أعظم فيلزمهم الكفر لكن فهم منكرين لذات المعبود الحق ولوجوده والمعتزلة في صفته لا في ذاته
The third chapter: On the one who affirms that God is a body, occupying space, and specifically associated with a particular direction — is he judged to be a disbeliever or not? Scholars have two opinions: The first: that he is a disbeliever — and it is the more apparent. This is because our doctrine is that everything that is specifically associated with a direction and space is created and originated, and it has a God who brought it into existence and created it. As for those who affirm body and direction, who deny the existence of any other existent besides these things that can be pointed to, they are deniers of the essence of the Existent which is believed to be God. And if they are deniers of His essence, they are disbelievers inevitably. This is unlike the Mu'tazila, for they affirm an existent beyond these things that are perceived by the senses, except that they disagreed with us about the attributes of that Existent. The anthropomorphists (mujassima) disagree with us about affirming the essence of the worshipped and His existence; this disagreement is greater. So disbelief necessarily applies to them, because they are deniers of the essence and existence of the true worshipped, while the Mu'tazila are deniers of His attributes, not of His essence.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the first opinion on the ruling of those who affirm that God is a body occupying space and direction. It begins by stating the opinion: such a person is a disbeliever, and this is the more apparent view. The proof is based on the premise that whatever is spatially located is created. Those who deny any existent beyond the physical are effectively denying God's very essence, hence disbelief. The passage then contrasts them with the Mu'tazila, who affirm a non-physical existent but err in attributes, so their error is less severe. The anthropomorphists are therefore disbelievers because they deny the essence, while the Mu'tazila only deny attributes.

Key Terms
تجسيم (tajsim)
مجسمة (mujassima)
جهة (jiha)
كفر (kufr)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Mu'tazila are also declared disbelievers in this passage.
Correction: The passage explicitly contrasts anthropomorphists with Mu'tazila, stating that the Mu'tazila only deny attributes, not essence, so they are not considered disbelievers here.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion, why are anthropomorphists considered disbelievers while the Mu'tazila are not?
A) Because both deny God's existence, but the Mu'tazila repent.
B) Because anthropomorphists deny the essence of God, while the Mu'tazila only deny some attributes.
C) Because anthropomorphists deny prophethood, while the Mu'tazila accept it.
D) Because the Mu'tazila affirm that God is a body but in a figurative sense.

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphists are disbelievers because they deny God's essence by affirming spatial location, whereas the Mu'tazila only deny attributes.

The passage distinguishes between denying God's essence and denying His attributes. Have you ever judged someone as a disbeliever over a disagreement that was actually about attributes? How does this distinction call you to examine your own tendency to takfir?
?
Razi's argument implies that to deny God's existence is clear disbelief, but to deny attributes is not. Does this make you uncomfortable? Are you willing to accept that some theological errors are more severe than others, and that God's mercy may extend to those who err on attributes?

Ibn Khuzayma's Literalism and Razi's Refutation
Editorial contrasts Ibn Khuzayma's literalism with Razi's methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conflict between Ibn Khuzayma's literalist theology and the metaphorical interpretation of divine attributes, and how this sets the stage for Razi's work on transcendence.

Core Idea
Ibn Khuzayma's anthropomorphism versus Razi's tanzih: a clash of methodologies.

This editorial note introduces a key figure – Ibn Khuzayma – whose literal reading of scripture led him to affirm physical attributes for God. His position directly opposes the rationalist approach of Imam Razi, who wrote Asas al-Taqdis to defend God's transcendence. Understanding this conflict helps us appreciate the stakes in classical kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the editorial note
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the full argument.
The note is external but provides important context.
2
Identify the anthropomorphic attributes
List the attributes Ibn Khuzayma affirms for God: face, hands, feet, etc.
He insists these are literal.
3
Understand the obligation to affirm a body
Ibn Khuzayma claims every believer must affirm that God is a body in the sense God affirmed for Himself.
This is a key difference from metaphorical interpretation.
4
Note his criticism of metaphorical interpretation
He accuses those who interpret metaphorically of ignorance and lack of depth.
This contrasts with the rationalist approach.
5
Reflect on Razi's opposition
The note ends by mentioning Razi's book, which refutes such literalism.
This sets the stage for Razi's defense of transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاحظ : قَضِيَّة الكتاب أولا : إن محمد بن إسحق بن خزيمة ، المتوفى سنة ٣١١ هـ - الذي يكرهه الإمام فخر الدين الرازى كرامة تحريم ، قد ألف كتابه « التوحيد وإثبات صفات الرب »، ليعلم الناس : أن الله - عز و جل – وجها ويدين وكلتا يديه يمين - ورجلين وأذنين وعينين . . . وهكذا . وأنه ـ عز وجل - يستوى على العرش في السماء . ومن لا يثبت ذلك الله عز وجل يعتبر كافرا . يقول ابن خزيمة : « واجب على كل مؤمن أن يثبت لخالقه وبارئه، جسم ، الله ، بمعنى [؟] ما أثبت الخالق البارى لنفسه ، من العين [؟]، وغير مؤمن من ينفى عن تبارك وتعالى - ما قد أثبته في محكم تنزيله (١) ، ومن [؟]، و من المسلمين من يفسر وجه الله ... إلخ هذه الصفات التى تو [؟] أن الله مجازي . كما تقول العرب : وجه الكلام ووجه الثوب . وابن خزيمة يصف هؤلاء المسلمين بالجهل، وبعدم التجرى [؟] في العلم . ويأتى بأحاديث كثيرة . ويفسرها على ظاهرها بدون تأويل . مثل : ( أتى التي [؟] صلى الله عليه وسلم رجل من أهل الكتاب ، فقال : يا أبا القاسم . أبلغك أن الله عز وجل يحمل الخلائق على إصبع ، والسموات على إصبع ، والأرضين على اصبع ، والشجر على إصبع ، والثرى على إصبع ؟ قال : فضحك النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حتى بدت نواجذه . قال : فأنزل الله تعالى : « وما قدروا الله حق قدره . والأرض جميعا قبضته يوم القيامة ، إلى آخر الآية . وقد ألف الإمام فخر الدين الرازي المتوفى في سنة ٦٠٦ ه كتابه
Observe: The thesis of the book: First: Indeed Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzayma, died 311 AH – whom Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi hates with a forbidden hatred – authored his book 'Al-Tawhid and Establishing the Attributes of the Lord', to teach people that God – Almighty – has a face, two hands, both hands right hands, two feet, two ears, two eyes... and so on. And that He – Almighty – sits on the Throne in heaven. And whoever does not affirm that for God Almighty is considered a disbeliever. Ibn Khuzayma says: 'It is obligatory upon every believer to affirm for his Creator and Maker, a body, God, meaning [؟] what the Creator and Maker has affirmed for Himself, of the eye [؟], and whoever denies from the Blessed and Exalted what He has affirmed in His clear revelation is not a believer (1), and whoever [؟], and among the Muslims are those who interpret the face of God... etc. these attributes which [؟] that God is metaphorical, as the Arabs say: the face of speech and the face of a garment. And Ibn Khuzayma describes these Muslims as ignorant, and lacking depth [؟] in knowledge. And he brings many hadiths and interprets them according to their apparent meaning without ta'wil. Such as: (A man from the People of the Book came to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and said: O Aba al-Qasim, has it reached you that God Almighty will carry the creatures on a finger, and the heavens on a finger, and the earths on a finger, and the trees on a finger, and the dust on a finger? He said: Then the Prophet, peace be upon him, laughed until his molars appeared. He said: Then God Almighty revealed: 'And they did not measure God with His true measure, while the earth all together will be in His grasp on the Day of Resurrection' to the end of the verse. And Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, died in 606 AH, authored his book...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an editorial note (لاحظ) that outlines the thesis of the book under discussion. It begins by referencing Ibn Khuzayma (d. 311 AH), a literalist theologian, whom Razi vehemently opposes. The note describes Ibn Khuzayma's book 'Al-Tawhid' in which he insists that God has a face, two hands, two right hands, feet, ears, eyes, and that He sits on the Throne physically. Ibn Khuzayma declares that whoever denies these anthropomorphic attributes is a disbeliever. He requires every believer to affirm that God is a body in the sense that God has affirmed for Himself, and he criticizes Muslims who interpret these attributes metaphorically, such as interpreting 'face of God' as the face of speech or the face of a garment (i.e., essence or direction). Ibn Khuzayma accuses these Muslims of ignorance and lack of depth in knowledge. He supports his view with hadith, including the famous hadith about God placing the heavens and earth on a finger, which he takes literally. The note contrasts this with Imam Razi's book, which is presumably 'Asas al-Taqdis', written to refute such anthropomorphism and establish God's transcendence. The note serves to highlight the polemical context of Razi's work.

التوحيد
الصفات
التنزيه
التأويل
التشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all early Muslim scholars were literalists in understanding God's attributes.
Correction: This note shows a clear division: Ibn Khuzayma's literalism was opposed by others, such as the those who used metaphorical interpretation, and ultimately by Razi who championed tanzih.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Khuzayma's literalism represents an extreme anthropomorphic theology that Razi's Asas al-Taqdis was written to refute.

The Multiple Meanings of 'Ibn' in Scripture
Ibn Taymiyyah explains the multiple meanings of 'ibn' in scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'ibn' in scriptures does not imply biological sonship but rather signifies love, selection, and mercy from God.

Core Idea
Scriptural 'son' means beloved, not begotten.

Ibn Taymiyyah unpacks the Arabic term 'ibn' (son) to show that it carries both a literal biological meaning and a metaphorical one. In scripture, when used for righteous individuals, it signifies God's love, selection, and mercy—not physical parentage. This lesson explores the linguistic and theological basis of this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of Ibn Taymiyyah's argument.
2
Identify the two usages of 'ibn'
Notice that Ibn Taymiyyah first distinguishes between literal birth and metaphorical causation. Find the examples he gives (ibn al-sabil, ibn al-ma').
These examples show how Arabic uses 'ibn' for things that 'come from' something else.
3
Connect to scriptural usage
See how he applies this to the Bible and Torah, where 'ibn' is used for righteous people. Understand that the intended meaning is love, selection, and mercy.
The prophets and their audiences understood this, but later followers misinterpreted it.
4
Reflect on the ambiguity
Consider why Ibn Taymiyyah calls this an ambiguous term (lafz mutashabih) and how misinterpretation led to false beliefs.
This reinforces the importance of proper hermeneutics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن تيمية : « لفظ الابن يعبر به عمن ولد الولادة المعروفة، و يعبر به عمن كان هو سببا فى وجوده . كما يقال ابن السبيل لمن ولدته الطريق فإنه لما جاء من جهة الطريق ، جعل كأنه ولده . ويقال لبعض الطير : ابن الماء ، لأنه يجى من جهة الماء. ويقال : كونوا من أبناء الآخرة ولا تكونوا من أبناء الدنيا . فإن الابن ينتسب إلى أبيه ويحبه ويضاف إليه. أي كونوا من ينتسب إلى الآخرة ويحبها ويضاف إليها . وهذا اللفظ موجود في الكتب التي بأيدى أهل الكتاب فى حق الصالحين الذين يحبهم الله ويربيهم. كما ذكروا أن المسيح قال : « أبي وأبيكم وإلهى وإلحكم ، وفي التوراة : أن الله قال ليعقوب : « أنت ابنى بكرى ، ونحو ذلك مما يراد به - إذا كان صحيحا ، اله معنى صحيح وهو - المحبة له والاصطفاء والرحمة له . وكان المعنى مفهوما عند الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - ومن يخاطبونه . وهو من الألفاظ المتشابهة ، فصار كثير من أتباعهم يريدون به المعنى الباطل (۲) اه.
Ibn Taymiyyah said: 'The term "ibn" (son) is used for one who is born in the well-known birth, and it is used for one who was a cause in his existence. Just as it is said "ibn al-sabil" (son of the road) for one whom the road gave birth to, because he came from the direction of the road, he was made as if he is its child. And it is said for some birds: "ibn al-ma'" (son of water), because it comes from the direction of water. And it is said: "Be among the sons of the Hereafter and do not be among the sons of the world." For the son is attributed to his father and loves him and is added to him. That is: be among those who are attributed to the Hereafter and love it and are added to it. And this term is found in the books that are in the hands of the People of the Book regarding the righteous whom God loves and nurtures. As they mentioned that the Messiah said: "My Father and your Father, and my God and your God," and in the Torah: that God said to Jacob: "You are My son, My firstborn," and similar [expressions] which are intended – if they are sound – a correct meaning, namely: Love for him, selection, and mercy for him. And the meaning was understood by the prophets – peace be upon them – and those they addressed. And it is among the ambiguous words. Then many of their followers intended by it the false meaning.' (2) End.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'ibn' (son) as used in scriptures, as presented by Ibn Taymiyyah. He begins by stating that the word 'ibn' has two basic usages: the literal biological son, and the metaphorical sense of being a cause or origin of something. He provides examples from Arabic language: 'ibn al-sabil' (wayfarer) meaning the road gave him birth, and 'ibn al-ma' (a water bird) meaning it comes from water. Then he gives the religious example: 'be sons of the Hereafter,' which means to be associated with the Hereafter in love and belonging. He then applies this to scriptural usage: in Jewish and Christian scriptures, the term 'ibn' is used for righteous people whom God loves and nurtures, such as Jesus calling God his Father (meaning beloved) and God calling Jacob his son. He explains that the intended meaning in these contexts is love, selection, and mercy, not biological fatherhood. He notes that this was understood by the prophets and their audiences, but later followers misinterpreted these ambiguous expressions (mutashabihat) to mean something false.

Key Terms
الابن (ibn - son)
اللفظ المتشابه (ambiguous term)
المحبة والاصطفاء (love and selection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'ibn' in scripture always implies biological sonship, implying God has a literal child.
Correction: Ibn Taymiyyah shows that 'ibn' can mean 'beloved' or 'one who is caused' in Arabic, and in scriptural contexts it signifies love, selection, and mercy, not biological fatherhood.

Checkpoint

A) biological relationship
B) love, selection, and mercy
C) prophecy
D) divinity

Key Takeaway
When scripture calls someone 'son' in relation to God, it means they are beloved, chosen, and shown mercy—not a biological offspring.

The Principle of Ta'wil for Anthropomorphic Verses
Explains the ruling of ta'wil for verses implying direction or body, with salaf and khalaf views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle of ta'wil for verses that imply direction or body, and the difference between the salaf and khalaf approaches to interpreting such texts.

Core Idea
Ta'wil of anthropomorphic verses is an obligation of divine transcendence, not a denial of attributes.

When the Quran or Sunnah uses language that might suggest Allah has a direction, body, or form, Muslims must interpret such passages in a way that upholds His transcendence. This lesson explains the agreed-upon ruling of ta'wil, using the example of the verse 'They fear their Lord above them' to illustrate the salaf and khalaf methodologies.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the structure: a general ruling followed by an example.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the exception for corporealists.
2
Understand the ruling on ta'wil
Identify that all Ahl al-Haqq agree that ta'wil is obligatory for verses implying direction, body, etc., except the mujassima and mushabbiha.
The reason given is the necessity of declaring Allah transcendent above the literal meaning.
3
Examine the example verse
Look at the verse 'They fear their Lord above them' and the two interpretations: salaf (tafwid: we do not know the modality) and khalaf (ta'wil: aboveness means exaltation in majesty).
Both approaches preserve transcendence by avoiding corporeal interpretations.
4
Reflect on the principle's importance
Consider why this principle is foundational for preserving tawhid and avoiding anthropomorphism.
Ta'wil is not a denial but a methodological tool to affirm Allah's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي أنه إذا ورد في القرآن أو السنة ما يشعر بإثبات الجهة أو الجسمية أو الصورة أو الجوارح، اتفق أهل الحق وغيرهم - ما عدا المجسمة والمشبهة - على تأويل ذلك، لوجوب تنزيهه تعالى عما دل عليه ما ذكر بحسب ظاهره. فما يوهم الجهة قوله تعالى: {يخافون ربهم من فوقهم} فالسلف يقولون: فوقية لا نعلمها، والخلف يقولون: المراد بالفوقية: التعالي في العظمة. فالمعنى: يخافون ربهم من أجل تعاليه في العظمة، أي ارتفاعه فيها... الخ
That is, when there occurs in the Quran or Sunnah what implies the affirmation of direction (jiha), corporeality (jismiyya), form (sura), or limbs (jawarih), the people of truth and others - except the corporealists (mujassima) and anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) - agreed on its ta'wil, due to the obligation of declaring Him transcendent above what that indicates according to its apparent meaning. As for what suggests direction, His statement: 'They fear their Lord above them.' The salaf say: 'A fawqiyya (aboveness) we do not know,' and the khalaf say: 'What is meant by fawqiyya is exaltation in majesty.' So the meaning is: They fear their Lord because of His exaltation in majesty, i.e., His loftiness therein... etc.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage explains the principle of ta'wil (interpretation) for verses and hadiths that might imply that Allah has a direction, body, form, or limbs. It states that all Muslims except the corporealists and anthropomorphists agree that such texts must be interpreted figuratively, because it is obligatory to declare Allah transcendent above what the literal wording suggests. It then gives an example: the verse 'They fear their Lord above them' (Quran 16:50). The salaf (early Muslims) say this 'above-ness' is something we do not know the modality of, while the khalaf (later scholars) interpret 'above' as meaning exaltation in majesty, i.e., it is a metaphorical aboveness referring to Allah's supreme greatness.

Key Terms
تأويل
جهة
جسمية
فوقية
سلف
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ta'wil means denying the attribute entirely, or that it is a form of allegorical interpretation that undermines the Quran's literal truth.
Correction: Ta'wil is not a negation; it is a methodological tool to uphold Allah's transcendence while affirming the attribute in a manner befitting Him. Both salaf and khalaf aim to protect tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the agreed-upon ruling on verses that suggest a direction for Allah?
A) They are to be taken literally.
B) They require ta'wil (interpretation).
C) They are abrogated.
D) They are only for the scholars.

Key Takeaway
The principle of ta'wil ensures that Allah is declared transcendent beyond any physical or directional attributes, while affirming His absolute greatness.

Al-Ghazali's Three Rules for Interpreting Anthropomorphic Expressions
Al-Ghazali presents three rules for handling anthropomorphic expressions and explains the two meanings of 'yad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's three rules for handling anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran and Hadith, and the two meanings of 'yad' (hand).

Core Idea
Interpretation has levels: commoners may remain silent, scholars must interpret.

Imam al-Ghazali lays down a clear methodology for dealing with verses and hadith that seem to describe Allah with physical features. He distinguishes between the obligations of common believers and scholars, and explains the word 'hand' as an example. This passage establishes a principle of affirming transcendence while allowing metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once without analyzing. Let the structure of the three rules and the explanation of 'yad' become clear.
2
Identify the three rules
List the three rules: (1) obligation to affirm transcendence from body and direction, (2) commoner's duty to first negate corporeality then interpret if possible, (3) scholars' collective obligation to interpret.
Notice that rule 2 allows the commoner to remain silent.
3
Understand the two meanings of 'yad'
Al-Ghazali explains that 'hand' has a literal meaning (a physical limb) and a metaphorical meaning (power, control). See how he uses the example of a city in the governor's hand.
4
Reflect on the central message
Consider why al-Ghazali emphasizes that the commoner is excused if unable to interpret. This protects faith while preventing anthropomorphism.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولا: يجب على جميع المسلمين أن ينزهوا الله من الجسمية والجهة.
ثانيا: إذا سمع العامي كلمة يد الله، فليعتقد أولا أن الله ليس جسما وليست له يد جارحة، وليفسر اليد ثانيا بأي معنى من المعاني التي تبعد اليد عن الجسمية كالقدرة مثلا. فإذا لم يحسن العامي أن يأتي اليد بمعنى يبعدها عن الجسمية فليسكت عن التفسير، وله عذر أمام الله.
ثالثا: التأويل واجب على العلماء في يد الله وسائر الأخبار التي توهم أن الله جسم، على سبيل فرض الكفاية. والعلماء في نظره هم أهل التصوف.
يقول الغزالي أبو حامد: إنه إذا سمع اليد والإصبع، وقوله: 'إن الله خلق طينة آدم بيده'، و'إن قلب المؤمن بين إصبعين من أصابع الرحمن'، فينبغي أن يعلم أن اليد تطلق على معنيين: أحدهما: هو الموضع الأصلي، وهو عضو مركب من لحم وعظم وعصب. واللحم والعظم والعصب: جسم مخصوص وصفات مخصوصة. وأعني بالجسم: عبارة عن مقدار له طول وعرض وعمق، يمنع غيره من أن يوجد بحيث هو إلا بأن يتنحى عن ذلك المكان.
الثاني: وقد يستعار هذا اللفظ - أعني اليد - لمعنى آخر، ليس ذلك المعنى بجسم أصلا، كما يقال: البلدة في يد الأمير. فإن ذلك مفهوم وإن كان الأمير مقطوع اليد مثلا.
فعلى العامي وغير العامي: أن يتحقق قطعا ويقينا أن الرسول - عليه السلام - لم يرد بذلك جسما هو عضو مركب من لحم ودم وعظم، وأن ذلك في حق الله تعالى محال وهو عنه مقدس. فإن خطر بباله أن الله جسم مركب من أعضائه، فهو عابد صنم... ومن نفى الجسمية عنه وعن يده وإصبعه، فقد نفى العضوية واللحم والعصب، وقدس الرب - جل عما يوجب الحدوث. وليعتقد بعده: أنه عبارة عن معنى من جلاله المعاني ليس بجسم ولا عرض في جسم، يليق ذلك المعنى بالله تعالى. فإن
First: It is obligatory upon all Muslims to declare Allah transcendent above corporeality and direction. Second: If the commoner hears the word 'hand of Allah', he should first believe that Allah is not a body and that He has no physical hand, and then interpret 'hand' with any meaning that distances it from corporeality, such as power. If the commoner cannot come up with a meaning that distances hand from corporeality, then he should remain silent about interpretation, and he has an excuse before Allah. Third: Interpretation is obligatory upon the scholars regarding the 'hand of Allah' and all other reports that suggest that Allah is a body, as a collective obligation (fard kifaya). The scholars in his view are the people of tasawwuf (Sufis). Al-Ghazali says: When one hears [the terms] hand and finger, and His statement: 'Indeed Allah created the clay of Adam with His hand,' and 'Indeed the heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Merciful,' one should know that 'hand' is used for two meanings: The first is the original place, which is a limb composed of flesh, bone, and nerve. Flesh, bone, and nerve are a specific body and specific attributes. By 'body' I mean an entity having length, width, and depth, which prevents something else from existing in its place except by moving away from that location. The second: This word - I mean 'hand' - may be used metaphorically for another meaning that is not a body at all, as it is said: 'The city is in the hand of the governor.' That is understandable even if the governor is, for example, missing a hand. Therefore, it is upon the commoner and the non-commoner to be absolutely certain that the Messenger - peace be upon him - did not intend by that a body that is an organ composed of flesh, blood, and bone, and that that is impossible with respect to Allah and He is transcendent above it. For if it crosses one's mind that Allah is a body composed of His parts, then he is a worshipper of an idol... And whoever negates corporeality from Him and from His hand and finger has negated organic composition and flesh and nerve, and has declared the Lord transcendent - exalted above what entails temporality. And let him believe afterwards that it [i.e., the hand] is an expression for a meaning from among His majestic meanings that is neither a body nor an accident in a body, befitting that meaning for Allah. For...
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Ghazali's approach to handling anthropomorphic expressions. It begins with three rules: First, all Muslims must affirm Allah's transcendence beyond body and direction. Second, if a common person hears 'hand of Allah', they should first affirm that Allah is not a body and then interpret 'hand' as power or similar; if they cannot interpret, they remain silent and are excused. Third, interpretation is a collective obligation on scholars. Al-Ghazali then elaborates on the word 'yad' (hand), explaining that it has two meanings: the literal meaning (a physical limb) and a metaphorical meaning (power or control). He uses the example of a city being in the governor's 'hand' even if the governor lacks a physical hand. He stresses that the Prophet did not intend a physical body, and that anyone who believes Allah has a physical body is akin to an idolater. He concludes by emphasizing that after negating corporeality, one should affirm that the 'hand' refers to a meaning befitting Allah that is not a body or accident. The passage ends abruptly with 'for...' indicating a continuation.

Key Terms
يد
تأويل
جسم
عرض
فرض كفاية
عامي
عالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the commoner is required to interpret anthropomorphic expressions even if unable.
Correction: Al-Ghazali explicitly states that if the commoner cannot find an appropriate interpretation, they should remain silent and have an excuse before Allah.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, what should a commoner do if he cannot find an appropriate interpretation for 'yad Allah'?
A) He must ask a scholar.
B) He must remain silent and is excused.
C) He must accept the literal meaning.
D) He must deny the hadith.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's method: affirm transcendence, interpret metaphorically when possible, and excuse the commoner from interpretation if unable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read or hear the phrase 'hand of Allah' in the Quran or hadith, do you immediately and sincerely affirm in your heart that Allah is not a body and has no physical limb? Or does a mental image of a hand slip in before you correct it? Starting today, what specific thought will you consciously replace that image with?
?
Al-Ghazali says that if a commoner cannot interpret, they are excused. But you are not a commoner—you are studying this lesson. What does that mean for your responsibility? Have you ever lazily avoided forming a proper understanding of Allah's attributes? What interpretation of 'yad' will you personally adopt and explain to others from now on?
?
The passage warns that thinking of Allah as a body composed of parts is akin to worshipping an idol. Search your heart: have you ever, even subtly, compared Allah to something physical or limited? How does that warning feel to you? What concrete step will you take today to purify your concept of Allah from any taint of corporeality?

The Obligation of Ta'wil and the Two Meanings of 'Hand'
Al-Ghazali states the obligation of ta'wil and explains two senses of 'hand'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ta'wil is a collective obligation for scholars and how Al-Ghazali distinguishes between the literal and metaphorical meanings of the word 'hand'.

Core Idea
Ta'wil is a collective duty for scholars to protect God's transcendence from anthropomorphism.

Al-Ghazali confronts a central challenge: how to understand scriptural descriptions of God's hand and fingers without falling into anthropomorphism. He asserts that scholars bear a collective responsibility to interpret these passages metaphorically. This lesson unpacks the two meanings of 'hand'—as a physical organ and as a metaphorical expression—and clarifies the role of scholars in safeguarding proper belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: Al-Ghazali first states the obligation, then explains the two meanings of 'hand'.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'hand'
Find the two meanings Al-Ghazali gives for 'hand'. Compare the literal meaning (body) with the metaphorical meaning (like 'city in the hand of the emir').
The literal meaning is defined in terms of a body with dimensions.
3
Understand the obligation of ta'wil
Reflect on why Al-Ghazali says this is a collective obligation (farḍ kifāyah). What would happen if no scholar performed ta'wil?
This prevents misunderstanding of God's nature.
4
Ask: Why is this important?
Consider how this passage distinguishes the role of scholars from common believers. Scholars must interpret; laypeople must only deny corporeality.
We will explore the layperson's duty in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثا : التأويل واجب على العلماء في [آية] يد الله ، وسائر الأخبار التي توهم أن الله جسم، على سبيل فرض الكفاية . والعلماء في نظره هم أهل التصوف . يقول الغزالي أبو حامد: إنه إذا سمع اليد والإصبع ، وقوله : إن الله خلق طينة آدم بيده ، و « إن قلب المؤمن بين إصبعين من أصابع الرحمن، فينبغي أن يعلم أن اليد تطلق على معنيين : أحدهما : هو الموضع الأصلي ، وهو عضو مركب من لحم وعظم وعصب . واللحم والعظم والعصب : جسم مخصوص ، وصفات مخصوصة. وأعني بالجسم : عبارة عن مقدار له طول و عرض و عمق ، يمنع غيره من أن يوجد بحيث هو : إلا بأن يتنحى عن ذلك المكان . الثاني : وقد يستعار هذا اللفظ - أعني اليد - لمعنى آخر ، ليس ذلك المعنى بجسم أصلا كما يقال : البلدة في يد الأمير . فإن ذلك مفهوم . وإن كان الأمير مقطوع اليد مثلا . فعلى العامي وغير العامي : أن يتحقق قطعا ويقينا أن الرسول - عليه السلام - لم يرد بذلك جسما، هو عضو مركب من لحم ودم وعظم، وأن ذلك في حق الله تعالى محال، وهو عنه مقدس . فإن خطر بباله : أن الله جسم مركب من أعضائه، فهو عابد صنم ... و من نفى الجسمية عنه وعن يده وإصبعه، فقد نفى العضوية واللحم والعصب، وقدس الرب - جل عما يوجب الحدوث . وليعتقد بعده : أنه عبارة عن معنى من المعاني ليس بجسم ولا عرض في جسم، يليق ذلك المعنى بالله تعالى . فإن كان لا يدري ذلك المعنى ولا يفهم كنه حقيقته فليس عليه في ذلك تكليف أصلا . فمعرفة تأويله ومعناه ليس واجبا عليه (١) ، ا هـ
Third: Interpretation is obligatory upon scholars concerning [the verse of] the hand of God, and all the reports that give the illusion that God is a body, as a collective obligation (farḍ kifāyah). The scholars in his view are the people of Sufism. Al-Ghazali Abu Hamid says: When one hears 'hand' and 'finger', and His saying: 'Indeed God created the clay of Adam with His hand', and 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Compassionate', he should know that 'hand' is used in two senses: One is the original place, and it is a composite organ of flesh, bone, and nerve. Flesh, bone, and nerve are a particular body and particular attributes. I mean by 'body': an expression for a magnitude having length, width, and depth, which prevents another from existing in its place unless it moves away from that location. Second: This word—I mean 'hand'—may be used metaphorically for another meaning that is not a body at all, as it is said: 'The city is in the hand of the emir', for that is understood, even if the emir is, for example, handless. So upon the common person and the non-common person is to ascertain absolutely and with certainty that the Messenger (peace be upon him) did not intend by that a body composed of flesh, blood, and bone, and that is impossible with respect to God, and He is sanctified from it. If it occurs to his mind that God is a body composed of His parts, he is a worshipper of an idol... And whoever denies corporeality concerning Him and concerning His hand and His finger has denied composition and flesh and nerve, and has sanctified the Lord—glorified is He above what necessitates temporality. And let him believe after that that it is an expression for a meaning among meanings that is not a body nor an accident in a body, befitting that meaning to God Almighty. If he does not know that meaning nor comprehend its reality, then there is no obligation upon him in that at all. So knowing its interpretation and its meaning is not obligatory upon him (1). End.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents the third point regarding the obligation of figurative interpretation (ta'wil) for scholars concerning anthropomorphic verses and hadiths. It states that this obligation is a collective duty (farḍ kifāyah). Then it quotes Al-Ghazali's detailed explanation. Al-Ghazali begins by identifying two meanings of 'hand': the literal, which refers to a physical organ composed of flesh, bone, and nerve (defining body as having length, width, depth, and occupying space), and the metaphorical, such as when we say 'the city is in the hand of the emir' even if the emir has no physical hand. He then rules that every person—commoner or scholar—must be certain that the Prophet did not intend a physical body by these terms, for that is impossible for God. If one thinks of God as a body, he becomes an idolater. To deny corporeality is to deny composition and sanctify God from temporal attributes. However, the common person is not required to know the exact nature of the intended meaning; he need only affirm that it is a meaning befitting God, not a body or accident. Thus, knowledge of the precise interpretation is not obligatory for the layperson. The quote ends here.

Key Terms
التأويل (ta'wil)
الجسم (al-jism)
اليد (al-yad)
فرض الكفاية (farḍ kifāyah)
المجاز (al-majāz)
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ghazali, what is the literal meaning of 'hand'?
A) A metaphorical expression for power
B) A physical organ with flesh and bone
C) A spiritual attribute
D) A symbol of creation

Key Takeaway
Ta'wil is a collective obligation for scholars, and the word 'hand' has both a literal (physical) and a metaphorical meaning, the latter being applicable to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses about God's 'hand', do you feel a subtle tug to imagine a physical hand? How can al-Ghazali's distinction between literal and metaphorical meanings help you train your heart to focus on God's power rather than forming a mental image?
?
Al-Ghazali says ta'wil is a collective obligation on scholars. If you are not a scholar, what is your personal responsibility when you encounter a verse that might be misunderstood? Will you ask a scholar, or will you risk falling into anthropomorphism by taking it literally?
?
Consider the phrase 'the city is in the hand of the emir' – even a handless emir can have a city in his hand. How does this example challenge any hidden assumption you might have that God's attributes must be like human attributes? What would it cost you to truly let go of that assumption?

What the Common Person Must Believe About God's Hand and Finger
Al-Ghazali explains the minimal belief required from common people regarding anthropomorphic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the minimum requirement for a common believer regarding anthropomorphic verses: denial of corporeality without needing to know the exact interpretation.

Core Idea
The common person need only deny that God is a body; knowing the exact ta'wil is not required.

Al-Ghazali clarifies a crucial distinction: while scholars must interpret anthropomorphic expressions, the common believer is only required to affirm that God is not a body. The common person is not burdened with understanding the precise metaphorical meaning. This lesson explains that obligation and protects against the misconception that everyone must be a scholar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the common person
Read the Arabic text that begins with 'فعلى العامي وغير العامي...' and the translation. Notice the immediate requirements.
2
Identify the two obligations
Find what the common person must do: 1) Ascertain that the Prophet did not intend a body, 2) Deny corporeality. And what they are not required to do: know the exact meaning.
The key is certainty about transcendence.
3
Understand the consequence of wrong belief
Al-Ghazali warns that believing God is a body amounts to idolatry. Reflect on the seriousness of this.
4
Differentiate between scholar and layperson
Contrast the duty of scholars (farḍ kifāyah to perform ta'wil) with the layperson's duty (only rejection of anthropomorphism).
This shows the mercy of Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that someone who imagines God as a body is like an idol worshipper, does your heart tremble? Have you ever caught yourself picturing God with form? What will you do to purify your belief starting today?
?
This passage frees you from needing to know the exact meaning of every verse about God's attributes. But does that freedom make you complacent? Are you content with just denying a body, or do you actively seek to know God through what He has revealed about Himself?
?
